Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
advanced
10 min
Definition of al-Wajib: Two Definitions and the Possibility of Pardon
Defines wajib and defends the possibility of pardon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi definitions of wajib and explain how each accommodates the possibility of divine pardon.

Core Idea
Wajib requires punishment, but pardon is possible because threats are not promises.

This passage from Izalat al-Rahbut presents two definitions of al-wajib (obligatory) as discussed in Musallam al-Thubut. The first definition emphasizes that the abandoner deserves punishment, either rationally or customarily. The second defines it as that which the abandoner is threatened with punishment. Both face the objection that they seem to preclude divine pardon. The commentator resolves this by clarifying that 'deserving' means suitability, not necessity, and that threats (wa'id) can be broken out of mercy, unlike promises (wa'd). Understanding this is essential for grasping Hanafi usul and its theological foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the first definition: 'that whose abandoner deserves punishment, a rational or customary deservingness.' Then the second: 'that which the abandoner is threatened with punishment.' Note the shift from 'deserving' to 'threatened'.
The first definition includes a qualifier (rational/customary); the second does not.
2
Analyze the objection to each definition
For the first: if one deserves punishment, then God must punish (contradicting pardon). For the second: if God threatens punishment, then pardoning would make the threat false (impossible for God).
Both objections assume an absolute necessity of punishment.
3
Examine the response to the first objection
The commentator says 'deserving punishment' here means suitability and customary expectation, not absolute obligation. Pardon is an act of generosity (karam) from God.
This draws on the Maturidi view that punishment is customary, not rationally necessary.
4
Examine the response to the second objection
The commentator distinguishes between wa'd (promise) and wa'id (threat). Breaking a promise is impermissible, but breaking a threat is permissible as an act of mercy.
Evidence: Quranic verses on forgiveness, intercession, and removal of sinners from fire.
5
Evaluate the final rebuttal and conclusion
A counter-argument claims God's threat is a report (khabar), which must be true. The author notes that if accepted, it would negate all pardon, which contradicts definitive texts. Thus the second definition remains valid with the wa'd/wa'id distinction.
The author's position: the second definition does not exclude pardon because threats can be broken.

Source Text

المسائل المتعلقة بالأحكام العملية

والآن نشرع في مسائل الأحكام ولنقدم عليها تعريف الواجب، وهو ما استحق العقاب تاركه استحقاقاً عقلياً أو عادياً والعفو من الكرم وقيل : هوما أوعد العقاب على تركه ولا يخرج العفو لأن الخلف في الوعيد جائز دون الوعد

يقول المصنف : والآن بعد الفراغ عن تعريف الحكم وبيان أقسامه من الأحكام التكليفية والوضعية نشرع في مسائل الأحكام، ولتقدم عليها على المسائل) تعريف الواجب ثم ذكر له تعريفين :
.1 تعريف الواجب الأول: (۱) وهو ما استحق تاركه العقاب استحقاقاً عقلياً، أو عادياً، وأما عدم العقاب والعفو عنه فهو ) من الكرم ، (أي من كرم الله تعالى على عباده). شرح التعريف : ولما كان حسن الأفعال وقبحها عقليين عند المعتزلة، وكانا مستلزمين للحكم عندهم عقلاً، وكان الحسن والقبح شرعيين عند الأشاعرة، وموجبين للحكم بعد ورود الشرع زاد المصنف قيد (عقلياً) ليشمل الواجب عند المعتزلة، وقيد (عادياً) ليشمل الواجب عند الأشاعرة والماتريدية، والماتريدية وان قالوا
بعقلية الحسن و القبح في بعض الأفعال، ولكنهم لم يقولوا باستلزام الحكم عقلاً، فعند الأشاعرة والماتريدية إنزال العقوبة على تارك الواجب أمر عادى عند الله تعالى، وليس
بواجب عقلاً، بل ان شاء عاقب، وان شاء عفا وغفر.
قوله : والعفو من الكرم: جواب عن إشكال يرد على قوله : (استحق تاركه العقاب) بأنّ المفهوم من استحقاق العقاب هو وجوب العقاب، سواء تاب أولم يتب، والأمر ليس كذلك، فانه قد يعفو الله تعالى بعد التوبة، وقد يعفو من فضله بغير توبة . فأجاب عنه بقوله : والعفو من الكرم، وحاصل الجواب أن المراد من الاستحقاق مجرد الصلاحية وإمكان العقاب لوجود الذنب بترك الواجب، أي يكون استحقاق العقاب عادةً، فانّ العادة أن المجرم يعاقب، وأما العفو (قبل التوبة أو بعدها) فهو من
کرمه تعالى .
(۲) وقيل (في تعريف الواجب الثاني ) ( هو ) ما أوعد بالعقاب على تاركه أي ما أنذر وخوّف تاركه بالعقاب ولما ورد الإشكال على ظاهره بأنه لا يدل التعريف على إمكان العفو، لأنه لو عفي تاركه لزم الكذب في خبر الوعيد بالعقاب واطلاق الكذب
على الله محال
أجاب عنه بقوله: ولا يخرج (إمكان) العفو لأن الخلف في (خبر) الوعيد جائز دون الوعد إذ الخلف في الوعيد عفو وكرم وسبق للرحمة، وليس بنقصان، وأما الخلف في الوعد بالنعمة فغضب، وترك للعفو، وعدم سبق الرحمة، وهذا نقص، تعالى الله عنه ومغفرة غير الشرك، وإخراج العصاة من النار، والشفاعة بأنواعها كلها دليل على جواز التخلف في الوعيد .
و رُدّ بأن إيعاد الله تعالى خبر، فهو صادق قطعاً، وتجويز كونه إنشاء للتخويف، كما قيل عدول عن الحقيقة، بلا موجب، على أن مثله يجرى في الوعد أيضا، فينسد باب المعاد
أقول : لو تم لدل على بطلان العفو مطلقاً، والكلام في خروجه بعد
The issues related to practical rulings. And now we begin the issues of rulings and we present before them the definition of al-wajib. It is that the abandoner of which deserves punishment, a rational or customary deservingness, and pardon is from generosity. And it is said: It is that the abandoner of which is threatened with punishment, and pardon is not excluded because breaking a threat is permissible unlike a promise. The author says: And now after finishing the definition of hukm and explaining its divisions from the taklifi and wad'i rulings, we begin the issues of rulings, and we present before them the definition of wajib, then he mentioned two definitions: 1. The first definition of wajib: It is that the abandoner of which deserves punishment, a rational or customary deservingness. As for the absence of punishment and pardon, it is from generosity, i.e., from God's generosity to His servants. Explanation of the definition: Since the goodness and badness of actions were rational according to the Mu'tazila, and they entailed the ruling rationally, and since goodness and badness are religious according to the Ash'aris and entail the ruling after the coming of revelation, the author added the qualification 'rational' to include wajib according to the Mu'tazila, and the qualification 'customary' to include wajib according to the Ash'aris and Maturidis. And the Maturidis, even though they say goodness and badness are rational in some actions, they do not say that the ruling is entailed rationally. So according to the Ash'aris and Maturidis, inflicting punishment on the abandoner of wajib is a customary matter from God, not rationally obligatory; rather, if He wills, He punishes, and if He wills, He pardons and forgives. His statement: 'And pardon is from generosity' is a response to an objection raised against his statement: 'the abandoner of which deserves punishment' — because the understanding from deserving punishment is the obligation of punishment, whether he repents or not, and the matter is not so, because God may pardon after repentance, and He may pardon out of His bounty without repentance. So he answered by saying: 'And pardon is from generosity'. The conclusion of the answer is that what is meant by deservingness is mere suitability and possibility of punishment due to the existence of sin by abandoning the wajib, i.e., the deservingness of punishment is customary, for the custom is that the criminal is punished. As for pardon (before or after repentance), it is from His generosity. (2) And it is said (in the second definition of wajib): It is that which the abandoner of which is threatened with punishment, i.e., the abandoner is warned and frightened with punishment. And when the objection is raised against its apparent meaning that it does not indicate the possibility of pardon, because if the abandoner were pardoned, it would necessitate falsehood in the report of threatening with punishment, and attributing falsehood to God is impossible. He answered by saying: And (the possibility of) pardon is not excluded because breaking (the report of) a threat is permissible unlike a promise. Since breaking a threat is pardon and generosity and precedence of mercy, and is not a deficiency. As for breaking a promise of goodness, it is anger, leaving pardon, and lack of precedence of mercy, and this is a deficiency — exalted is God above that. And the forgiveness of anything other than shirk, and the removal of sinners from the Fire, and intercession of all types, all are evidence for the permissibility of non-fulfillment in the threat. And it was replied: That God's threatening is a report, so it is certainly true, and allowing it to be a declaration for intimidation, as is said, is a deviation from the truth without cause, and likewise applies to promises as well, thus closing the door of return (i.e., resurrection). I say: If it were valid, it would prove the nullification of pardon absolutely, and the discussion is about its exclusion after...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This is a key passage in Hanafi usul al-fiqh defining the term 'wajib' (obligatory). The author (al-Badakhshani) is commenting on the matn of Musallam al-Thubut. He presents two definitions of wajib and defends them against objections. The first definition: wajib is that whose abandoner deserves punishment, either rationally (as Mu'tazila hold) or customarily (as Ash'aris and Maturidis hold). The author adds the qualification 'rational' and 'customary' to accommodate different schools within Sunni Islam. He then addresses the objection that 'deserving punishment' seems to preclude pardon. The response is that 'deserving' here means suitability and customary expectation, not absolute necessity; pardon is an act of generosity from God. The second definition: wajib is that which the abandoner is threatened with punishment. Here, the objection is that if God threatens punishment, then giving pardon would make the threat false. The response distinguishes between a threat (wa'id) and a promise (wa'd); breaking a threat is permissible and is a sign of mercy, whereas breaking a promise is impermissible. The passage then records a rebuttal that God's threat is a report, which must be true, so pardon would be impossible. The author concludes with his own comment that if that rebuttal were accepted, it would negate all possibility of pardon, which contradicts the teachings of the Quran and hadith about forgiveness and intercession. Thus, the correct view is that the second definition also allows for pardon.

Key Terms
الواجب (al-wajib): That which the abandoner deserves punishment for abandoning, either rationally or customarily, with the possibility of pardon.
الوعيد (al-wa'id): A threat of punishment; breaking it is permissible as an act of mercy.
الوعد (al-wa'd): A promise of reward; breaking it is impermissible and a deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the first definition of wajib from the second according to the text?
A) The first mentions punishment, the second does not.
B) The first includes a qualification about rationality or custom, while the second defines it as being threatened with punishment.
C) The first allows for pardon, the second does not.
D) The first is for Mu'tazila, the second for Ash'aris.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of wajib allows for divine pardon because deserving punishment is customary, not absolute, and threats can be broken out of mercy.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Second Definition of Wajib: The Issue of Forgiveness
Analysis of the second definition of wajib with objections and rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that divine threats (waʿīd) are true reports but allow for forgiveness, and evaluate the rebuttals against the construction interpretation.

Core Idea
Wajib is defined by its threat of punishment, but that threat is a true report not negated by divine forgiveness.

In this passage, the author analyzes the second definition of wajib and tackles a crucial theological-legal problem: if wajib is defined by a threat of punishment, does forgiveness make the threat false? The Hanafi-Maturidi response preserves both the truth of the threat and the possibility of divine mercy, distinguishing between threats and promises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second definition and its apparent implication
The passage presents the second definition: 'Wajib is what is threatened with punishment for its abandoner.' At face value, this seems to imply that punishment must inevitably follow abandonment.
This definition is common in kalām discussions, especially among Hanafis and Maturidis.
2
Understand the objection: forgiveness would entail lying
If God forgives an abandoner, the threat would not be fulfilled, leading to a false report (khabar) from God, which is impossible. This objection challenges the coherence of the definition.
The objection assumes threats are unconditional reports.
3
Examine the initial defense: discrepancy in threat is permissible as generosity
The author defends the definition by arguing that discrepancy (khulf) in a threat is permissible as an act of generosity and mercy, unlike in a promise, where discrepancy would be a deficiency.
This defense relies on a distinction between threats and promises.
4
Analyze the rebuttal: threats are true reports, not constructions
A strong rebuttal is presented: the threat is a true report (khabar), not a mere construction (inshā') for intimidation. Treating it as inshā' would require abandoning the literal meaning without evidence, and would also apply to promises, thereby abolishing the necessity of the afterlife.
The Quranic verse 4:93 is cited to emphasize the truthfulness of threats.
5
Synthesize the correct position: threat qualified by forgiveness
The correct Hanafi position is that the threat is a true report but is qualified by the possibility of forgiveness. The discrepancy (khulf) is permissible because it is an act of mercy, not a falsehood.
This preserves both the seriousness of the obligation and the mercy of God.

Source Text

بعقلية الحسن و القبح في بعض الأفعال، ولكنهم لم يقولوا باستلزام الحكم عقلاً، فعند الأشاعرة والماتريدية إنزال العقوبة على تارك الواجب أمر عادى عند الله تعالى، وليس بواجب عقلاً، بل ان شاء عاقب، وان شاء عفا وغفر.
قوله : والعفو من الكرم: جواب عن إشكال يرد على قوله : (استحق تاركه العقاب) بأنّ المفهوم من استحقاق العقاب هو وجوب العقاب، سواء تاب أولم يتب، والأمر ليس كذلك، فانه قد يعفو الله تعالى بعد التوبة، وقد يعفو من فضله بغير توبة . فأجاب عنه بقوله : والعفو من الكرم، وحاصل الجواب أن المراد من الاستحقاق مجرد الصلاحية وإمكان العقاب لوجود الذنب بترك الواجب، أي يكون استحقاق العقاب عادةً، فانّ العادة أن المجرم يعاقب، وأما العفو (قبل التوبة أو بعدها) فهو من كرمه تعالى .
(۲) وقيل (في تعريف الواجب الثاني ) ( هو ) ما أوعد بالعقاب على تاركه أي ما أنذر وخوّف تاركه بالعقاب ولما ورد الإشكال على ظاهره بأنه لا يدل التعريف على إمكان العفو، لأنه لو عفي تاركه لزم الكذب في خبر الوعيد بالعقاب واطلاق الكذب على الله محال
أجاب عنه بقوله: ولا يخرج (إمكان) العفو لأن الخلف في (خبر) الوعيد جائز دون الوعد إذ الخلف في الوعيد عفو وكرم وسبق للرحمة، وليس بنقصان، وأما الخلف في الوعد بالنعمة فغضب، وترك للعفو، وعدم سبق الرحمة، وهذا نقص، تعالى الله عنه ومغفرة غير الشرك، وإخراج العصاة من النار، والشفاعة بأنواعها كلها دليل على جواز التخلف في الوعيد .
و رُدّ بأن إيعاد الله تعالى خبر، فهو صادق قطعاً، وتجويز كونه إنشاء للتخويف، كما قيل عدول عن الحقيقة، بلا موجب، على أن مثله يجرى في الوعد أيضا، فينسد باب المعاد
أقول : لو تم لدل على بطلان العفو مطلقاً، والكلام في خروجه بعد تسليم وجوده فلابد أن يقال إن الإيعاد في كلامه تعالى مقيد بعدم العفو فقال : ورُدَّ (هذا الدليل أولاً بأن ابعاد الله تعالى (وتخويفه) خبر، فهو صادق قطعاً، فلا يكون مثل وعيد العبد لمجرد الترهيب والتخويف) قال تعالى (ومن يقتل مؤمنا متعمداً فجزاء، جهنم خالدا فيها وغضب الله عليه ولعنه واعد له عذاباً أليما)
ولولم يكن هذا الوعيد خبراً صادقاً لما كانت الحاجة إلى هذه التاكيدات البليغة ؟ و (أما) تجويز كونه انشاء (لمجرد) التخويف والترهيب، كما قيل (في الجواب عن هذا الدليل فهو ) عدول عن الحقيقة ( وهو كونه خبراً) بلا موجب (أي بلا دليل موجب ترك الحقيقة، والعدول عنها إلى المجاز وهو الإنشاء.
وثانياً أنه علا أي يزيد ويعلو على الرد الأول رد (آخر) وكلمة (علا) ليست. حرف جرّ في مثل هذا الموضع، بل هي فعل من على يعلو بمعنى العلو والفوق، أي يجينى فوق الرد الأول رد آخر ، وهو أن مثل هذا التأويل أي) كون الخبر بمعنى الانشاء، وهو انشاء الطمع والترغيب) يجرى في الوعد (أيضاً)
وإذا اكان الخبر في الوعيد بمعنى انشاء التخويف، وفي الوعد بمعنى انشاء الترغيب والطمع، ولم يكن ثواب ولا عقاب لم تبق الضرورة إلى قيام القيامة، فينسد باب إثبات المعاد وعود الحياة الثانية، ولم تبق الحاجة إلى يوم المعاد.
(وثالثاً) أقول (في الرد ) لو تم ذلك الدليل وهو كون خبر الوعيد بمعنى انشاء التخويف ) لدل على بطلان العفو مطلقاً، سواء كان قبل التوبة أو بعدها يعنى إذا لم يكن العقاب أصلاً ؛الأجل أن الوعيد لمجرد التخويف، لا للعقاب في الواقع، فلا يستحق العبد العقاب فلا يكون عقاب ولا عفو، فلا حاجة (بعد التعريف الثاني للواجب الى قوله : " ولا يخرج العفو وإنما يكون الكلام في خروجه (العفو) (وعدم خروجه بعد تسليم وجوده باستحقاق العقاب ، وإذا سقط العقاب بتأويل الوعيد بالانشاء لم يبق للعفو محل، فكيف يصح قوله : ( ولا يخرج العفو)؟
ولمارة المصنف الأدلة الثلاثة لجواز الخلف في الوعيد أشار إلى الدليل الصحيح
With the rational faculty of good and evil in some actions, but they did not say that the ruling is necessitated rationally. So according to the Ash'aris and Maturidis, inflicting punishment on the abandoner of the wajib is a customary matter with Allah, not rationally obligatory. Rather, if He wills, He punishes, and if He wills, He pardons and forgives.
His statement: 'And forgiveness is from generosity' is a response to an objection raised against his statement: 'The abandoner deserves punishment'. The objection is that the understanding from deserving punishment is the obligation of punishment, whether he repents or not, but the matter is not like that, for Allah may forgive after repentance, and may forgive out of His bounty without repentance. So he answered by saying: 'And forgiveness is from generosity'. The summary of the answer is that what is meant by 'deserving' is mere suitability and possibility of punishment due to the existence of the sin of abandoning the wajib, i.e., deserving punishment is customary, for the custom is that the criminal is punished. As for forgiveness (before repentance or after it), it is from His generosity.
(2) And it was said (in the second definition of wajib): (It is) what punishment is promised for its abandoner, i.e., what its abandoner is threatened and warned with punishment. When the objection arose against its apparent meaning that the definition does not indicate the possibility of forgiveness, because if its abandoner is forgiven, it would entail lying in the report of the threat of punishment, and attributing lying to Allah is impossible.
He answered by saying: 'And the possibility of forgiveness is not excluded' because discrepancy in the report of threat is permissible, unlike promise. For discrepancy in threat is forgiveness, generosity, and precedence of mercy, and is not a deficiency. As for discrepancy in the promise of favor, it is anger, abandonment of forgiveness, and lack of precedence of mercy, and this is a deficiency — Allah is exalted above that. And forgiveness of other than shirk, and removal of sinners from the Fire, and intercession in all its forms, are all evidence for the permissibility of discrepancy in threat.
And it was rebutted that Allah's threatening is a report, so it is absolutely true. And considering it to be a construction for intimidation, as was said, is a departure from the literal meaning without cause, given that the same applies to promise as well, so the door of resurrection would be closed.
I say: If it were valid, it would indicate the absolute invalidity of forgiveness, and the discussion is about its inclusion after admitting its existence. So it must be said that the threat in Allah's speech is qualified by the absence of forgiveness. So he said: 'This proof was rebutted firstly by saying that Allah's threatening (and intimidation) is a report, so it is absolutely true, so it is not like the threat of a slave for mere intimidation and deterrence.' Allah said: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein, and Allah has wrath upon him and has cursed him and prepared for him a great punishment' (4:93).
If this threat were not a true report, why would there be a need for these emphatic assertions? As for considering it a construction (for mere) intimidation and deterrence, as was said (in response to this proof), it is a departure from the literal meaning (it being a report) without cause (i.e., without proof necessitating leaving the literal meaning and deviating to the figurative, which is the construction).
Secondly, that 'ala' means it increases and rises above the first rebuttal (another) rebuttal, and the word 'ala' is not a preposition in such a place, but a verb from 'ala ya'lu' meaning elevation and supremacy, i.e., there comes above the first rebuttal another rebuttal, which is that such an interpretation (the report meaning construction, i.e., construction of desire and inducement) applies also to the promise.
And if the report in threat means construction of intimidation, and in promise means construction of inducement and desire, and there is no reward nor punishment, then there remains no necessity for the establishment of the Hour, so the door of proving the resurrection and the return of the second life would be closed, and there would be no need for the Day of Resurrection.
(And thirdly) I say (in refutation): If that proof (i.e., the report of threat meaning construction of intimidation) were valid, it would indicate the absolute invalidity of forgiveness, whether before repentance or after, meaning if there is no punishment at all, because the threat is for mere intimidation, not for punishment in reality, so the servant does not deserve punishment, so there is no punishment nor forgiveness. So after the second definition of wajib, there would be no need for his statement: 'And forgiveness is not excluded'. Rather, the discussion is about its inclusion (forgiveness) and non-inclusion after admitting its existence through deserving punishment. And if punishment is removed by interpreting the threat as construction, there remains no place for forgiveness, so how can his statement 'And forgiveness is not excluded' be valid?
And it may be that the author, through the three proofs for the permissibility of discrepancy in threat, pointed to the correct proof.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses the second definition of wajib (obligatory) and the theological question of whether divine threats of punishment necessarily entail actual punishment, or whether forgiveness (afw) remains possible. The discussion is situated within Ash'ari/Maturidi kalām, where punishment is not rationally obligatory on God. The author begins by explaining that the first definition's claim 'the abandoner deserves punishment' is interpreted as mere suitability, not inevitability, allowing for forgiveness from God's generosity. Then the second definition is introduced: 'Wajib is what is threatened with punishment for its abandoner.' An objection arises: if forgiveness occurs, the threat becomes false, which is impossible for Allah. The response defends forgiveness by distinguishing between threats (waʿīd) and promises (waʿd): discrepancy (khulf) in threats is permissible as an act of generosity, while in promises it would be a deficiency. Evidence includes forgiveness of sins other than shirk and intercession.

The author then presents a rebuttal (radd) to this defense. The rebuttal argues that Allah's threats are true reports (khabar), not mere constructions (inshā') for intimidation. Treating them as constructions is a departure from the literal meaning without justification, and it would also apply to promises, thereby negating the need for resurrection and reward/punishment. A Quranic verse (4:93) is cited to emphasize the truthfulness of threats. Three further points are made: (1) the threat is a true report, so it cannot be reinterpreted as a construction; (2) if the threat were a construction, the same would apply to promises, abolishing the necessity of the afterlife; (3) if the threat is merely for intimidation and not actual punishment, then no punishment is deserved, leaving no room for forgiveness at all. Thus, the only coherent position is that the threat is a true report qualified by the possibility of forgiveness (i.e., God's mercy may override it). The author hints that the correct proof among the three earlier arguments is the last one (allowing discrepancy in threat while maintaining it as a true report). This analysis is crucial for Hanafi uṣūl because the definition of obligatory acts (wajib) directly impacts legal obligation and the consequences of non-compliance.

Key Terms
الواجب (al-wājib): What is threatened with punishment for its abandoner (second definition); a legally binding command whose violation warrants punishment in the afterlife.
الوعيد (al-waʿīd): A divine threat of punishment for disobedience; a report (khabar) that is normally true but can be overridden by forgiveness.
الخبر (al-khabar): A statement that can be true or false; divine threats are considered true reports (khabar) in principle, not constructions (inshā').
الإنشاء (al-inshāʾ): A speech act that creates a legal situation (e.g., promise, threat as a performative); here considered a departure from the literal meaning if applied to divine threats.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the second definition of wajib does not exclude the possibility of forgiveness?
A) Because the definition itself explicitly states that forgiveness is allowed.
B) Because discrepancy (khulf) in a threat is permissible as generosity, unlike in a promise.
C) Because the Quranic threats are merely construction (insha') for intimidation.
D) Because forgiveness eliminates the need for punishment entirely.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the definition of wajib via threat of punishment does not negate divine forgiveness because the threat is a true report qualified by mercy, not an unconditional execution.

The Collective Duty (Fard Kifaya) in Hanafi Usul
Definition and ruling of fard kifaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish fard kifaya from fard 'ayn and explain the Hanafi view that it is wajib on every individual.

Core Idea
Fard kifaya is obligatory on every individual; performance by some drops the obligation, but this is not abrogation.

This passage defines a key category of obligation in Hanafi usul: the collective duty (fard kifaya). Understanding its structure is essential for differentiating between personal and communal obligations in Islamic law. The text also clarifies a common confusion about abrogation (naskh) and provides the proof for individual obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Read the first sentence: fard kifaya is obligatory on every single person but is dropped by the act of some. Highlight the phrase 'waajib 'ala al-kull'.
This is the core definition.
2
Understand the ruling mechanism
The obligation falls when some perform it. This is not naskh because the command remains; the cause (the need) is removed. Note the two reasons given.
Compare with the example of zakat falling when nisab is destroyed before the year.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The proof is the consensus that if everyone knows no one performed it, they all sin. This shows the obligation was on each individual. Trace the logic: sin on all → obligation on all.
This is a 'dalil al-ijma' (proof from consensus).
4
Contrast the alternative view
Some hold that the obligation is on an ambiguous group (ba'd mubham). The text rejects this because if it were on some, not all would sin when the duty is abandoned.
This highlights the practical consequence of the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الواجب (الفرض الكفائي وابهام المكلف الواجب على الكفاية واجب على الكل أي كل واحد ويسقط بفعل البعض، ولا يلزم النسخ؛ لأن سقوط الأمر قبل الأداء قديكون لانتفاء علة الوجوب، وقيل : هو واجب على البعض، لنا إثم الكل بتركه إذا ظنوا أن غيرهم لم يفعل وفي محل وجوب الفرض الكفائي مذهبان : ۱ - الأول : أن الوجوب على كل فرد (وحكمه أنه يسقط الواجب بفعل البعض و أدائه، عن الآخرين، فلا يأثمون بتركه، كصلاة الجنازة والجهاد، ولا يلزم أن يكون ترك عمل البعض بذلك الواجب نسخ وجوب العمل عليه قبل العمل به لوجهين : الأول: إنما يكون النسخ بالمنع عن العمل، ولم يمنع الشرع البعض الآخر عن العمل به والثاني : أن سقوط العمل بالأمر قبل الأداء قد يكون لانتفاء علة الوجوب، كسقوط وجوب الزكوة لأجل هلاك النصاب قبل الحول، أو سقوط الصلاة عن الحائض بسبب عدم طهارتها . ۲ - والثاني : أنه قيل إن الوجوب أيضاً على البعض المبهم (كما أن الأداء على البعض غير المعين). ودليلنا (دليل الجمهور ) على الوجوب على كل فرد فرد أنهم إذا علموا أن أحداً لم يعمل بالواجب على الكفاية بأثم كلهم، ولو كان الوجوب على البعض لم يأثم كلهم .
Issue: The obligatory (collective duty and ambiguity of the obligated). The collective duty is obligatory upon everyone, i.e. every single person, and it is dropped by the action of some. And abrogation is not necessary because the fall of the command before performance may be due to the absence of the cause of obligation. And it was said: it is obligatory upon some. For us, the sin of all if they leave it when they think that others did not do it. Regarding the place of obligation of collective duty, there are two schools: First: that the obligation is upon every individual (and its ruling is that the obligatory is dropped by the action of some and its performance, from others, so they do not sin by leaving it, like the funeral prayer and jihad. And it is not necessary that the abandonment of action by some regarding that duty be abrogation of the obligation of action upon them before acting on it, for two reasons: First: abrogation only occurs by prohibition of action, and the Law did not prohibit others from acting on it. Second: that the fall of action due to the command before performance may be due to the absence of the cause of obligation, like the fall of the obligation of zakat due to the destruction of the nisab before the year, or the fall of prayer from the menstruating woman due to her lack of purity. Second: that it was said that the obligation is also upon the ambiguous some (just as the performance is upon some unspecified person). And our proof (the proof of the majority) for the obligation upon each individual is that if they know that no one has performed the collective duty, they all sin. And if the obligation were upon some, not all would sin.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This is a definitional passage on fard kifaya (collective obligation) from the Hanafi usul perspective. It states that fard kifaya is obligatory on every single individual ('ala kulli wahid), but it is dropped when some perform it. The author clarifies that this is not abrogation (naskh), as abrogation requires prohibition, while here the command remains but the obligation falls due to removal of the cause. Two opinions are given: the first (Hanafi view) says the obligation is on each individual; the second (weaker) says it is on an ambiguous group. The proof for the first is that if everyone knows no one has performed it, they all sin – this shows the obligation was on each. Examples include funeral prayer and jihad. The falling of obligation before performance is likened to zakat falling due to destruction of nisab, or prayer falling for a menstruating woman – it is not naskh but a natural expiration of the cause.

Key Terms
الفرض الكفائي (al-fard al-kifa'i): A collective obligation that must be performed by at least some, but initially incumbent on all
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation of a legal ruling by a later revelation
البعض المبهم (al-ba'd al-mubham): An unspecified or ambiguous group of people

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the dropping of obligation in fard kifaya NOT considered naskh (abrogation)?
A) Because naskh only applies to prohibitions
B) Because the command remains but the cause of obligation is removed
C) Because the obligation is automatically annulled when time passes
D) Because naskh requires a new revelation

Key Takeaway
Fard kifaya is wajib on every individual initially, but performance by some removes the obligation from others without abrogation.

The Unspecified Part and Sin in Collective Obligations
Defining wajib kifai and resolving sin paradox and child's prayer objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi resolution of the sin paradox in wajib kifai and apply the analogy of volunteer payment to the child's janazah prayer.

Core Idea
Sin in wajib kifai falls on the whole, not the unspecified part, avoiding punishment of an unknown entity.

This passage from Ibn al-Humam's Tahrir al-Usul tackles a logical puzzle: if a collective obligation (wajib kifai) is imposed on an unspecified part of the community, how can that part be punished for omission when it is undefined? The author presents a refined Hanafi solution that distinguishes between two 'individuals' of that part, and then defends the view against a Shafi'i objection involving a child's janazah prayer. You will learn the precise mechanism by which the whole community bears the sin, and why the child's valid act drops the obligation from adults.

Guided Path
1
Identify the paradox in wajib kifai
The obligation is on an unspecified part (ba'd mubham). If everyone omits it, how can the unspecified part be punished? That would be punishing an unknown entity, which is irrational.
This is the core logical problem the passage solves.
2
Understand the two 'individuals' of the unspecified part
The unspecified part has two individuals: one is a subset of the part (some people), the other is the whole (all people). The first is too vague to punish; the second (the whole) is concrete and can bear sin.
This distinction allows sin to be attributed without logical contradiction.
3
Analyze the Qur'anic proof and the Hanafi reinterpretation
Opponents cite verse 9:122 to say obligation is on the part. The Hanafi response: the verse indicates that the obligation drops by the action of some, not that it is imposed on the part. The hadith 'seeking knowledge is fard on every Muslim' shows it is on all, so reconciling both texts means the duty is collective but sin is on all if all neglect.
The author uses 'jama' bayn al-adilla' (reconciliation of proofs).
4
Address the child's janazah prayer objection
Shafi'is argue that a discerning child's performance cannot drop the obligation from adults because the child is not obligated. Hanafi analogy: a volunteer paying a debt fulfills the creditor's purpose; similarly, the Lawgiver's purpose (honoring the deceased) is achieved by the child, so the obligation drops.
This is a key application of maqasid (purpose) in usul.
5
Apply the Hanafi position to a new scenario
Consider a different wajib kifai, e.g., washing a deceased Muslim. If a discerning child performs it, does it drop from adults? According to the Hanafi analogy, yes, because the purpose (cleaning the body) is fulfilled.
Test the analogy's scope.

Source Text

The unspecified part (the obligatory). Yes, the unspecified is not inclusive of the whole, meaning it is not rationally understandable. The summary of this statement consists of points: First: The unspecified part upon which the obligation lies and upon which sin rests in case of omission has two individuals: one of them exists within that part (some individuals), and the other exists within the whole (all individuals). The first individual of that part is unspecified and its punishment in case of omission is not rationally conceivable, while the second individual (which is the whole) does not contradict the attainment of sin due to omission, and punishing the second individual (the whole) for leaving the unspecified part is rational, so that punishing and tormenting the unspecified definite is not necessitated. Second: His saying: 'When the whole performs it, etc.' is evidence for his saying: 'The whole is among the individuals of the unspecified part.' Its summary is that when all the obligated persons perform the collective obligation, it is performance of what was obligatory upon them by agreement, and the obligation was upon the unspecified part, and performance by the whole occurred, so it is known that the whole's performance of the obligation is an individual instance of the part's performance, just as the sin of the part falls away by its falling away from the whole. Thus, by parallelism it is established that the sin of the whole is an individual instance of the sin of the unspecified part. Third: The unspecified part that does not include its other individual (the whole) is not rationally conceivable to be punished, because it entails tormenting the unspecified; but in the case where the unspecified part includes the whole, there is no tormenting of the unspecified, so it is rational. Fourth: In his saying: 'So reflect' there is an indication of the persistence of the original problem, namely punishing the unspecified part per se, while punishing the whole is secondary and incidental, so it is disregarded. Thirdly: Allah Most High said: 'So why not go forth from every group?' We say: (the mentioned) is interpreted as falling by the action of the part, reconciling the evidences. And they (those who say the obligation is on the part and sin on the part in case of omission) said thirdly: (in their argument they said) Allah Most High said: 'So why not go forth from every group among them a party to gain understanding in religion?' The detail of the argument is that seeking knowledge is a collective obligation, 'group' is a large company, and 'party' is a small company. So He commanded some of the large company to seek knowledge, which is religious knowledge. Thus it is known that the collective obligation is upon an unspecified part, and sin also is upon that part. The author said in response to this argument of theirs: We say: (His saying) is interpreted as falling by the action of the part, reconciling the evidences, i.e., the verse does not indicate obligation upon the part, but rather indicates the falling of the obligation by the action of the part. This indication is for two reasons: First: The whole is punished for leaving the part, and if the obligation were on the part, the whole would not be punished for leaving the part. Second: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim.' The word 'every' explicitly indicates obligation upon all. This is the meaning of his saying 'reconciling the evidences,' meaning the Book and the Sunnah. Then in 'al-Tahrir' there is a problem regarding the janazah prayer dropping by the action of a discerning child, as is the more correct view among the Shafi'is, even though there is no obligation upon him. I say: There is no problem, for that is like the dropping of a debt by the payment of a volunteer. Then the author pointed to a problem in the definition of collective obligation and said: In 'Tahrir al-Usul li-Ibn al-Humam' there is a problem: the definition of collective obligation is problematic due to the dropping of the janazah prayer (which is a collective obligation) by the performance of a discerning child, as is the more correct view among the Shafi'is, even though there is no obligation upon him. For the intended person who drops the obligation from the whole by his action is one upon whom that obligation is due, i.e., an adult, and a discerning child is not like that, yet when a discerning child performs the janazah prayer, it drops from the adults. So he pointed to the answer by saying: I say: There is no problem? For that (dropping of janazah prayer by the performance of a discerning child) is like the dropping of a debt by the performance of a volunteer. For the purpose of the creditor in the case of debt is the attainment of his money, and that has been found. Similarly, the purpose of the Lawgiver in the command of janazah prayer is the fulfillment of the right of the Muslim and honoring him, and burying him after praying over him, and this objective has been achieved by the prayer of the discerning child.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of collective obligations (wajib kifai) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, focusing on the paradox of sin and punishment when the obligated party is an unspecified individual. The text presents four points clarifying that although the obligation falls on an unspecified part (ba'd mubham), the sin for omission is actually on the 'whole' (kull) — that is, the comprehensive individual that encompasses all possible obligated persons. This avoids the problem of punishing an unknown entity. The author then addresses an objection raised by those who argue that sin should be on the unspecified part, citing the Quranic verse (9:122) which seems to command a group to seek knowledge. The response reinterprets this verse to indicate that the obligation drops by the action of some, not that it is specifically on an unspecified part, supported by the hadith 'seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim.' Finally, the text resolves a counterexample from the Shafi'is: the janazah prayer performed by a discerning child drops the obligation from adults, even though the child is not obligated. The Hanafi answer compares this to a volunteer paying a debt: the creditor's purpose is fulfilled regardless of who pays, and similarly, the Shariah's purpose (honoring the deceased) is achieved by the child's prayer, so it is valid.

Key Terms
الواجب الكفائي (al-wajib al-kifa'i): A collective obligation; a duty that, if performed by some, is dropped from all obligated persons.
البعض المبهم (al-ba'd al-mubham): The unspecified part; an indeterminate set of individuals upon whom the initial obligation is believed to fall in wajib kifai.
الكُل (al-kull): The whole; the entire body of obligated individuals, which is considered an individual of the unspecified part and the locus of sin in case of omission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Hanafi school attribute sin for omission of a wajib kifai to the whole (al-kull) rather than the unspecified part?
A) Because the unspecified part cannot be punished as it is not a concrete entity.
B) Because the whole is the only one who can be obligated.
C) Because the verse 9:122 explicitly commands the whole.
D) Because the unspecified part is always innocent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi understanding of wajib kifai resolves the paradox of punishing an unknown part by attributing sin to the whole, and uses purpose-based analogy to allow a child's valid act to discharge the communal duty.

Wajib Kifai and the Child's Prayer Objection
Refining wajib kifai through an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of wajib kifai to explain why a discerning child's action can fulfill a communal obligation, and identify the role of the Lawgiver's purpose in determining fulfillment.

Core Idea
Wajib kifai is fulfilled when the Lawgiver's purpose is achieved, even by a non-mukallaf.

This passage refines the definition of communal obligation (wajib kifai) by addressing an objection from Ibn al-Humam's Tahrir. The objection claims that if a discerning child performs the funeral prayer—a wajib kifai—the obligation falls from the community, even though the child is not legally obligated (mukallaf). The commentator resolves this by showing that the definition hinges on fulfilling the Lawgiver's purpose, not on the legal capacity of the performer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
Recognize that the funeral prayer is classified as a communal obligation (wajib kifai) in the passage.
Wajib kifai is an obligation on the community as a whole; if some perform it, the rest are absolved.
2
State the problem from al-Tahrir
Note the objection: A discerning child (sabi 'aqil) is not mukallaf, yet his performance of the funeral prayer causes the obligation to fall from the adults. This seems to contradict the definition that the performer must be the obligated person.
3
Analyze the commentator's response
The commentator uses an analogy: just as a volunteer paying someone else's debt discharges the debt because the creditor's purpose (receiving money) is met, the child's prayer fulfills the Lawgiver's purpose (honoring the deceased).
The key is the maqsad (purpose) of the Lawgiver, not the legal capacity of the actor.
4
Extract the refined definition
Wajib kifai is an obligation demanded of all mukallafin but falls by the action of any person—mukallaf or not—if the intended purpose (maslahah) is achieved.
This aligns with Hanafi usul's emphasis on maqasid.

والطائفة الجماعة القليلة، فأمر بعض الجماعة العظيمة، بطلب العلم وهو علم الدين، فعلم أن الواجب الكفائي يكون على بعض غير معين، والاثم أيضا يكون على ذلك البعض. قال المصنف في الجواب عن دليلهم هذا : قلنا : (قوله تعالى مأول بالسقوط بفعل البعض جمعاً بين الأدلة، أي لا تدل الآية على الوجوب على البعض، بل تدل على سقوط الواجب بفعل البعض، وهذه الدلالة لوجهين : الأول : تأثيم الكل بترك البعض، ولو كان الوجوب على البعض لم يأثم الكل بترك البعض والثاني قال النبي : طلب العلم فريضة على كل مسلم فلفظ كل صريح في الوجوب على الكل . وهذا معنى قوله ( جمعاً بين الأدلة) يعنى الكتاب والسنة . ثم في "التحرير" يشكل بسقوط الجنازة بفعل الصبي العاقل، كماهو الأصح عند الشافعية مع أنه لا وجوب عليه أقول : لا إشكال، فإن ذلك كسقوط الدين بأداء المتبرع ثم أشار المصنف إلى إشكال في تعريف الواجب الكفائي وقال : في "تحرير الأصول لابن الهمام) يشكل تعريف الواجب الكفائي بسقوط صلاة الجنازة (التي هو فرض كفائي) باداء الصبي العاقل كما هو الأصح عند الشافعية) مع أنه لا وجوب عليه، فإن المراد من البعض الذي يسقط الواجب عن الكل بفعله) هو من وجب عليه ذلك الواجب أي البالغ والصبي العاقل ليس كذلك، مع أن صلاة الجنازة إذا أذاها الصبي العاقل تسقط عن البالغين. فأشار إلى الجواب بقوله : أقول : لا إشكال ؟ فإن ذلك (سقوط صلاة الجنازة بأداء الصبي العاقل كسقوط الدين بأداء (الشخص المتبرع فإنّ غرض الدائن في صورة الدين هو حصول ماله فقد وجد . وكذلك غرض الشارع من الأمر بصلاة الجنازة هو أداء حق المسلم وإكرامه، ودفنه بعد الصلاة عليه، وقد حصل هذا المقصد بصلاة الصبي العاقل .
And the limited group, so He commanded some of the great group to seek knowledge, which is the knowledge of religion, so it is known that the communal obligation is upon some unspecified [person], and the sin is also upon that some. The author said in response to this evidence: We say: (His saying, may He be exalted, is interpreted as falling by the action of some, to reconcile the evidences, i.e., the verse does not indicate obligation upon some, but rather indicates that the obligation falls by the action of some. This indication is from two aspects: first, the blaming of all for abandoning it by some, and if the obligation were upon some, all would not be blamed for the abandonment of some. Second, the Prophet said: Seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim, so the word 'every' is explicit in obligation upon all. This is the meaning of his statement 'reconciling the evidences', meaning the Book and the Sunnah. Then in al-Tahrir it is problematic due to the funeral prayer falling by the action of a discerning child, as is the more correct [view] among the Shafi'is, while there is no obligation upon him. I say: There is no problem, for that is like the falling of a debt by the payment of a volunteer. Then the author pointed to a problem in the definition of the communal obligation and said: In Tahrir al-Usul by Ibn al-Humam, the definition of the communal obligation is problematic due to the funeral prayer (which is a communal obligation) falling by the performance of a discerning child, as is the more correct [view] among the Shafi'is, while there is no obligation upon him. For the intended meaning of 'the one who causes the obligation to fall from all by his action' is the one upon whom that obligation is imposed, i.e., the adult, and the discerning child is not like that, yet when the discerning child performs the funeral prayer, it falls from the adults. So he indicated the response by saying: I say: There is no problem? For that (the falling of the funeral prayer by the performance of the discerning child) is like the falling of a debt by the payment of a volunteer, for the creditor's purpose in the case of a debt is to obtain his property, which has been obtained. And similarly, the Lawgiver's purpose in commanding the funeral prayer is to fulfill the right of the Muslim and honor him, and bury him after the prayer upon him, and this objective has been achieved by the prayer of the discerning child.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definition of communal obligation (wajib kifai). The author begins by citing a Quranic verse (likely 9:122) which, according to the commentator, indicates that the obligation falls by the action of some, not that it is imposed on some. Two proofs are given: first, if the obligation were only on some, the entire community would not be blamed for its abandonment by that some; second, the hadith 'Seeking knowledge is an obligation upon every Muslim' explicitly places the obligation on all. Then an objection from al-Tahrir is raised: the funeral prayer, a communal obligation, is fulfilled by a discerning child (sabi 'aqil) who is not legally obligated (mukallaf), which seems to contradict the definition that the one performing must be the one obligated. The commentator responds with an analogy: a volunteer paying a debt causes the debt to be discharged even though he was not obligated to pay; similarly, the purpose of the Lawgiver (honoring the deceased) is achieved by the child's prayer, so the obligation falls. The author then seconds this, noting that the definition should not require the performer to be the obligated person. Thus, wajib kifai is fulfilled when the intended purpose (maslahah) is realized, regardless of the performer's legal capacity. This follows the Hanafi usul methodology of considering the objective of the Law (maqasid).

Key Terms
الواجب الكفائي (al-wajib al-kifa'i): ما طلب الشارع فعله من المكلفين بحيث يسقط الإثم عن الكل بفعل البعض، ويحصل المقصد الشرعي حتى بفعل غير المكلف
الصبي العاقل (al-sabi al-'aqil): الطفل المميز الذي لم يبلغ، لكنه يعقل ويميز الأفعال، وهو غير مكلف بالإجماع
المتبرع (al-mutabarri'): من يتبرع بأداء الدين أو العبادة دون أن يكون ملزما به

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A slave being freed by a non-owner
B) A volunteer paying off someone else's debt
C) A child inheriting from a parent
D) A person performing wudu for another

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of wajib kifai centers on achieving the Lawgiver's purpose, not on the legal capacity of the performer.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Definition and Four Opinions on the Non-specific Obligatory Act (Wajib Mukhayyar)
Defines واجب مخير and presents four juristic opinions on its nature

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-wajib al-mukhayyar, distinguish the four scholarly opinions on its nature, and apply the Hanafi/majority position to an expiation scenario.

Core Idea
Wajib mukhayyar obligates one unspecified alternative; performing any one fulfills the duty.

The concept of a non-specific obligatory act may seem puzzling: how can a command be valid if it does not specify exactly what to do? This lesson unpacks the definition of al-wajib al-mukhayyar, the obligatory act with alternatives, as illustrated by the expiation for an oath. You will examine four scholarly opinions on what exactly is obligated, see the Hanafi refutation of the weaker views, and understand why the majority position is that a single unspecified alternative suffices.

Guided Path
1
Define al-wajib al-mukhayyar
Identify the core elements: an obligatory act with multiple specified alternatives; the obligator requires the servant to choose and perform any one of them. Note the example of expiation (kafarah) alternatives.
The definition emphasizes the validity of commanding an unspecified matter from a known set.
2
Identify the four opinions
List the four views presented: (1) obligation of one unspecified alternative (majority/Hanafi), (2) obligation of all alternatives, with sufficiency of one (attributed to some Mu'tazila), (3) obligation of a specific alternative determined by God but unknown to the servant, (4) obligation of a specific alternative that does not vary but can be fulfilled by any option.
The author considers views 2 and 3 as weak or problematic.
3
Analyze the Hanafi refutation of views 2 and 3
See how the author critiques view 2 by noting that if all were obligatory, performing one would leave the others unperformed, and gathering all may be impossible (e.g., appointing one imam). For view 3, the refutation is that obligation must precede the act; if specification only occurs after the act, obligation would also be after, which is invalid.
The logical requirement that a command precedes the commanded act is central.
4
Apply the majority opinion to a concrete scenario
Consider a person who breaks an oath. The expiation is to feed ten poor people, clothe them, or free a slave. According to the Hanafi/majority position, he may choose any one of the three; his obligation is fulfilled by that single act. He is not required to do all three.
This illustrates the practical consequence (thamarah) of the ikhtilaf.

عليه
The obligated person. Issue 2: The non-specific obligatory act and ambiguity in what is required, the act obligated. It is valid to obligate an act from known matters, and it is the non-specific obligatory act (in terminology) like the expiation alternatives. Definition of non-specific obligation: It is obligating an unspecified matter from multiple matters from which one chooses, like obligating one of the three matters in expiation, which are mentioned in the Quran as His statement: 'So its expiation is feeding ten needy people from the average of what you feed your families, or clothing them, or freeing a slave.' And therein (regarding whether the obligation from the multiple matters is all of them or some of them) there are four schools. First: It is the school of the majority that the obligation is one unspecified from those matters. For this, the author said in its definition: 'Obligating a matter from known matters is valid, and it is the non-specific obligatory act (in terminology) like the expiation alternatives,' meaning obligating some and performing it is valid. And it was said: Obligation of all, and it falls by doing some; if one does all, he deserves the reward of multiple obligations. I say that is a branch of the permissibility of gathering all, and it might not be permissible, like appointing one of those qualified for leadership. Then this probability is not known for certain. Second: It was said it is obligation of all the multiple matters and falls by doing some. Then the author criticized it and said: If the obligated person does all, he deserves the reward of multiple obligations because he did all that was commanded. I say: that (deserving reward for obligations) is a branch of the permissibility of gathering all, and it might not be permissible (the gathering) like appointing one of those qualified for leadership. Appointing the leader is obligatory; if there are many qualified for supreme leadership, they cannot gather and appoint more than one, otherwise they would rebel against each other and cause corruption on earth. And he said secondly: Then this probability of obligation of all and clearing of liability by doing some is not well-known so as to be considered and answered. It has been attributed to some Mu'tazila. And it was said: It is specific with Him Almighty, and it is what the slave does. So it varies. And it was refuted that obligation must be before the act so that it can be complied with. So understand. Third: That the obligation (in the non-specific duty) is one of the multiple matters, and it is specific with God Almighty, and it is what the slave does, and ambiguous for the slave before the act, it becomes specific for him after ability and action, thus it varies. That is, that ambiguous obligatory matter varies according to the variation of the obligated persons; some perform feeding upon oath-breaking, some clothe, some free. It does not mean that being specific with God is that each obligated person comes with one specific matter from those matters, but whatever the obligated person does is specific with God. The author weakened the third definition by saying: It was refuted that obligation is only before the act so that it can be complied with, i.e., so that the obligated person fears the abandonment of obligation and prepares to comply. The source of the refutation and objection is the statement that specification for the slave only occurs after the act and performance, and obligation only materializes after specification, so obligation would be established after the act, despite that obligation must be specific before the act so that the obligated person can comply with it. The author said in his commentary: Know that obligation is a demand, and demand only occurs before the existence of the demanded act, and specification (even if it is in the knowledge of God Almighty) is a branch of existence, because knowledge follows the known (existent). And in his saying 'so understand' there is indication of the delicacy and importance of the topic: that this is relative to other than Him Almighty. As for His knowledge, it is before and after existence, and His known is dependent on His knowledge. And it was said: It is specific and does not vary, but it is fulfilled by it and by another, and we have rational permissibility and the text indicates it.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the definition and juristic analysis of the 'non-specific obligatory act' (الواجب المخير). The author begins by stating that it is valid to obligate a matter from among known options, and that this is the واجب مخير, exemplified by the expiation alternatives (خصال الكفارة) in the Quran (feeding, clothing, freeing a slave). He then presents the core issue: when multiple alternatives are given, is the obligation all of them, some, or one? He reports four scholarly views (مذاهب). The first view, and that of the majority (الجمهور), is that the obligation is one unspecified act from among the alternatives. This is the standard Hanafi position: the obligated person must fulfill one of the options, not all. The second view (attributed to some Mu'tazila) is that all alternatives are obligatory, but performing one suffices to discharge the duty. The author critiques this by pointing out that if one does all, would he deserve multiple rewards? That depends on whether gathering all alternatives is possible, which is not always the case (e.g., appointing a single imam; you cannot appoint multiple simultaneously). He further notes that this view is weak and not well-known. The third view is that the obligatory act is one specific alternative determined by God but unknown to the slave until after the act. The author refutes this by arguing that obligation must precede the act for it to be a valid command; if specification only occurs after the act, then obligation would also be after, which is illogical. The fourth view (briefly mentioned) is that it is a specific act that does not vary (i.e., it is fixed which alternative is obligatory for each person), but it can be fulfilled by any alternative (implying that the specific one is unknown to us). The author states that this is rationally permissible and supported by textual evidence. Throughout, the author emphasizes that obligation (طلب) must be before the act, and that God's knowledge encompasses all possibilities, but our human obligation is to choose one. The Hanafi position, following the majority, is that the obligation is one unspecified alternative—this is the relied-upon view for practice (e.g., in expiation, you choose one of the three).

Key Terms
الواجب المخير (al-wājib al-mukhayyar): An obligatory act where the Lawgiver offers multiple alternatives, and the obligated person is required to choose and perform one of them (e.g., expiation alternatives).
إيجاب المخير (ījāb al-mukhayyar): The act of obligating an unspecified matter among multiple known alternatives, such that the obligated person may choose any one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the opinion of the majority (الجمهور), what is the nature of the obligation in a non-specific obligatory act (الواجب المخير)?
A) All alternatives are obligatory individually.
B) One specific alternative is obligatory as determined by God.
C) One unspecified alternative is obligatory from among the set.
D) The obligation is suspended until the slave decides which to do.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on al-wajib al-mukhayyar is that it obligates one unspecified alternative, a view supported by Quranic text and logical coherence.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Third Position on Wajib Mukhayyar and Its Refutation
Defines the third position on the obligated act in mukhayyar wajib and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the third position on the nature of the obligated act in wajib mukhayyar and explain the logical refutation against it.

Core Idea
In wajib mukhayyar, the obligated act is divine-determined but servant-ambiguous until performance — but obligation cannot be post-act.

When multiple options are given for fulfilling an obligation (e.g., kaffarah), what exactly is the wajib? Is it a specific act known only to Allah, or any one of the options? This passage explores the third position that attempts to reconcile divine determinacy with human choice, but a key refutation exposes a fatal flaw: obligation is a demand that must precede the act, so the wajib cannot become determined only after the act. The author adds a subtle note about the nature of knowledge and demand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim of the third position
Read the first sentence: the wajib is one of the multiple things, determined in Allah's sight but ambiguous to the servant before the act. After ability and performance, it becomes determined for him.
The key distinction: determination (ta'yin) in Allah's knowledge vs. for the servant.
2
Distinguish divine and human determination
The text clarifies: Allah's determination is that whatever the servant does is known as the specific wajib; it is not that every servant must perform one specific act. Each mukallaf's actual performance is what is determined in Allah's knowledge.
This avoids the conclusion that the wajib is a single predetermined act for all.
3
Understand the refutation's logic
The refutation states that obligation (wujub) must exist before the act so that the servant can comply and fear leaving it. But the third position makes determination (and thus obligation) occur after the act, which contradicts the nature of obligation as a prior demand.
The phrase 'even yamaththal' (so that he complies) indicates the purpose of prior obligation.
4
Analyze the author's added commentary
The author explains: obligation is a demand (talab), and demand exists before the existence of the demanded. Determination in Allah's knowledge, however, follows existence because knowledge follows the known (for other than Allah). But Allah's knowledge encompasses both before and after.
This distinguishes created knowledge (which depends on the object) from divine knowledge (which precedes and creates).
5
Note the alternative opinion mentioned at the end
Another view holds that the wajib is a specific determined act that does not vary, but it is fulfilled by that act or another. The author says this is rationally permissible and indicated by the text.
This alternative avoids the problem by asserting a fixed wajib that is fulfilled by any of the options (a kind of 'specification by substitution').

Source Text

والثالث: أن الواجب (في الواجب المخير) أحد الأمور المتعددة، وهو معين عند الله تعالى، وهو ما يفعله العبد ، ومبهم عند العبد قبل الفعل، فيتعين عنده بعد القدرة عليه ،والفعل، فيختلف أي يكون ذلك الأمر الواجب المبهم عند العبد مختلفاً باعتبار اختلاف المكلفين، فبعضهم يأتى بالاطعام عند الحنث، وبعضهم يأتى بالكسوة، وبعضهم يأتى بالتحرير، وليس معنى كونه معيناً عند الله أن كل واحد من المكلفين يأتي بامر واحد معين من تلك الأمور، بل كلّما يأتى به المكلف فهو معين عند الله . وضعف المصنف التعريف الثالث بقوله : ورُدّ بأن الوجوب (إنما) يكون قبل الفعل حتى يمتثل ، أي حتى يخاف المكلّف عن ترك الواجب فهيأ نفسه للامتثال به. ومنشأ الرد والاعتراض هو القول بأن التعيين عند العبد إنما يحصل بعد الفعل والاتیان به والوجوب إنما يتحقق بعد التعيين، فكذلك الوجوب يثبت بعد الفعل مع أنه لا بد أن يكون الوجوب متعيناً قبل الفعل حتى يمتثل به المكلف. قال المصنف في تعليقه : اعلم أن الوجوب طلب، والطلب إنما يكون قبل (وجود) المطلوب بالفعل ، و ( أما ) التعيين (وإن كان في علم الباري تعالى ففرع الوجود؛ لأن العلم تابع للمعلوم (الموجود). وفي قوله ( فافهم) إشارة إلى دقة المقام وأهميته، وهو أن هذا بالنسبة إلى غيره تعالى، وأما علمه تعالى فيكون قبل الوجود وبعده ، و يكون معلومه تعالى تابعاً لعلمه وقيل : معين لا يختلف، لكن يسقط به وبالآخر، ولنا الجواز عقلاً والنص دال عليه
And the third: that the obligated (in the mukhayyar wajib) is one of the multiple things, and it is determined in the sight of Allah Almighty, and it is what the servant does, and ambiguous for the servant before the act, so it becomes determined for him after ability and the act, so it varies meaning that that obligated matter ambiguous for the servant differs according to the difference of the obligated persons, so some of them perform feeding upon expiation, some perform clothing, and some perform freeing, and the meaning of it being determined with Allah is not that every single obligated person comes with one specific matter from those matters, but rather whatever the obligated person performs is determined with Allah. And the author weakened the third definition by saying: and it was refuted that obligation (only) occurs before the act so that he complies, i.e., so that the obligated person fears leaving the wajib and prepares himself to comply with it. And the origin of the refutation and objection is the statement that determination for the servant only occurs after the act and performance, and obligation only occurs after determination, so similarly obligation is established after the act, while obligation must be determined before the act so that the obligated person can comply with it. The author said in his commentary: Know that obligation is a demand, and demand only occurs before the existence of the demanded in actuality, and as for determination (even if it is in the knowledge of the Creator, it is a branch of existence because knowledge follows the known (existent). And in his saying (so understand) is an indication of the subtlety of the topic and its importance, and it is that this is relative to other than Him, but as for His knowledge, it is before existence and after it, and His known is dependent on His knowledge. And it was said: it is specific, not varying, but it is fulfilled by it and by another, and for us permissibility rationally and the text indicates it.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents the third position regarding the nature of the obligated act in a mukhayyar wajib (optional obligatory act). The author defines this position: the wajib is one of the multiple options, determined in Allah's knowledge but ambiguous to the servant before the act. After the servant performs the act, it becomes determined for him. The author then reports the refutation of this definition: obligation must precede the act for compliance, but here determination happens after the act, implying obligation also occurs after the act, which contradicts the requirement that obligation be determined before the act. The author adds his own commentary: obligation is a demand, which must precede the existence of the demanded; determination in Allah's knowledge is dependent on the existence of the act because knowledge follows the known. However, this applies only to created beings; Allah's knowledge encompasses both before and after existence. The passage concludes by noting another opinion: the wajib is a specific determined act that does not vary, and it is fulfilled by that specific act or another, and this is rationally permissible and indicated by the text.

Key Terms
الواجب المخير (al-wājib al-mukhayyar): An obligatory act where the mukallaf has a choice between multiple specified options, e.g., kaffarah options.
التعيين (al-ta'yīn): The determination or specification of the wajib either in Allah's knowledge or for the servant.
الوجوب (al-wujūb): The property of being obligatory; the legal obligation itself.
الطلب (al-ṭalab): Demand; the imperative aspect of obligation that must precede the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the third position on wajib mukhayyar considered refuted?
A) It contradicts the Quranic text on kaffarah.
B) It implies that obligation occurs after the act, but obligation must precede the act.
C) It denies that Allah knows which act will be performed.
D) It requires the servant to know the wajib before acting.

Key Takeaway
The third position on wajib mukhayyar is refuted because it makes obligation posterior to the act, contradicting the essential nature of obligation as a prior demand.
