Condition for Delaying a Broadly-Timed Obligation: Hanafi Response to an Objection
Refuting an objection to delaying a broadly-timed wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that conjecture (zann) of safety is the condition for delaying a broadly-timed wajib and analyze the logical structure of the rebuttal against the objection that requires actual safety.

Core Idea
Safety of outcome is not the condition for delay; only conjecture is required, otherwise the expansive time becomes constrained.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection that would make delaying a broadly-timed obligation impossible. The objector argues that since only Allah knows whether one will survive until the end of the time, the condition for permissible delay must be actual safety, not knowledge. The Hanafi response dismantles this by showing it leads to an impossible choice and nullifies the very nature of expansive time. Let's unpack the rebuttal step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that if the condition for delay is knowledge or conjecture of safety, it leads to taklif bi al-muhal (obligating the impossible). Therefore, the condition must be actual safety of outcome.
This initial objection appears plausible but will be shown as flawed.
2
State the Hanafi counter: takhyir bayna mumkin wa mumtani'
The objection is invalid because it forces a choice between a possible action (delay with conjecture) and an impossible action (delay with knowledge). This is called takhyir bayna mumkin wa mumtani'.
A choice between a possible and an impossible action is not a valid legal option.
3
Understand the two interpretations of 'possible' and 'impossible'
First interpretation: 'possible' = delay based on conjecture; 'impossible' = delay based on knowledge of the unknown. Second interpretation: 'possible' = delaying if one survives; 'impossible' = delaying if one dies. Both show the absurdity of the objection.
The second interpretation highlights that actual safety cannot be a condition because it is unknown.
4
Recognize the consequence: expansive time destroyed
Accepting the objection would remove the expansive nature of the time, making it constrained, which contradicts the premise of a broadly-timed wajib.
If delay were only permissible with certainty, the time effectively becomes an immediate obligation.
5
Identify the second answer: condition is zann
The author adds that we do not accept that safety of outcome is the condition; rather, the condition is conjecture (zann), which is neither impossible nor beyond human capability.
This final answer directly refutes the objection by clarifying the actual condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقول بان شرط الجواز سلامة العاقبة لا العلم به حق يؤدي إلى التكليف بالمحال هذا اعتراض على جواز تاخير الواجب مع ظن السلامة ، بأنه لو كان شرط جواز تاخير الواجب العلم أو الظن بالسلامة يلزم التكليف بالمحال؛ لأن العلم بسلامة المكلف وعدم سرعة موته عنده تعالى، فإنه غيب وعالم الغيب هو الله تعالى، والعبد لا يعلم بسلامته شيئاً، فعلم أن شرط جواز تاخير الواجب هو سلامة العاقبة (أي سلامة عاقبة المكلف حتى يمكنه الأداء في آخر الوقت لا العلم ( أو الظن) بسلامته عن الموت، حتى يؤدي و يفضي إلى التكليف بالمحال . فهذا القول أو الاعتراض غير صحيح، فإنه يقتضي التخيير بين ممكن وممتنع ، وحاصل الرد أن سلامة العاقبة لا تكون شرطاً الجواز تاخير الواجب ، وإلا لزم التخيير بين الممكن والممتنع ، بين ظن السلامة والعلم بسلامة العاقبة . والمراد بالممكن هو جواز التاخير مع ظن السلامة ، وبالممتنع هو جواز التاخير مع العلم بسلامة العاقبة وقد ذكرنا أن علمها خاص بالله تعالى . وقيل المراد بالممكن على تقدير عدم الموت في الوقت، وبالممتنع على تقدير الموت فيه وهو ( التخيير بين الممكن والممتنع ) يرفع حقيقة التوسع ، ولم يبق وقت الواجب موسعاً ، بل صار مضيقاً مع أن الكلام في وقت الواجب الموسع. وقوله : فتدبر : فيه إشارة إلى جواب آخر، وهو أنا لا نسلم أن شرط جواز التاخير هو سلامة العاقبة حتى يلزم التكليف بالمحال ) وهو العلم بسلامة العاقبة بل شرط جواز التاخير هو الظن بسلامة العاقبة ، وهذا ليس بمحال، ولا يُكلف به.
And the statement that the condition for permissibility is safety of outcome, not knowledge of it, leads to obligating the impossible. This is an objection to the permissibility of delaying the obligation with conjecture of safety: that if the condition for permissible delay were knowledge or conjecture of safety, it would necessitate obligating the impossible, because knowledge of the safety of the obligated person and the absence of his sudden death is with Him, exalted; for it is the unseen, and the Knower of the unseen is Allah Almighty, and the servant does not know anything of his safety. So it is known that the condition for permissible delay is safety of outcome (i.e., the safety of the outcome of the obligated person so that he can perform at the end of the time), not knowledge (or conjecture) of safety from death, such that it leads to obligating the impossible. So this statement or objection is incorrect, because it necessitates choosing between a possible and an impossible thing. The gist of the response is that safety of outcome is not a condition for permissible delay of the obligation; otherwise it would necessitate choosing between the possible and the impossible, between conjecture of safety and knowledge of safety of outcome. And what is meant by the possible is the permissibility of delay with conjecture of safety, and by the impossible is the permissibility of delay with knowledge of safety of outcome, and we have mentioned that knowledge of it is specific to Allah Almighty. And it is said that what is meant by the possible is assuming not dying in the time, and by the impossible is assuming dying in it. And that (choosing between the possible and the impossible) removes the reality of expanse, and the time of the obligation does not remain expansive, but becomes constrained, while the discussion is about the expansive time of the obligation. And his saying: so ponder: in it is an indication of another answer, and it is that we do not concede that the condition for permissible delay is safety of outcome such that it necessitates obligating the impossible (and it is knowledge of safety of outcome); rather, the condition for permissible delay is conjecture of safety of outcome, and this is not impossible, nor is one obligated to do it.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and rebuttal of an objection regarding the condition for delaying a broadly-timed obligation (wajib musawwa). The objection states: if the condition for permissible delay were knowledge or conjecture of safety from death, then it would be an impossible obligation (taklif bi al-muhal) because knowledge of safety is only with Allah. The objector concludes that the real condition is safety of outcome itself, not knowledge or conjecture. The Hanafi response is that this objection is invalid because it forces a choice between a possible action (delay with conjecture) and an impossible one (delay with knowledge). Safety of outcome cannot be the condition for permissible delay because it is unknown; rather, the actual condition is conjecture (zann) of safety. The text further explains two meanings of 'possible' and 'impossible': one based on the nature of knowledge, and another based on the assumption of life or death. The second interpretation shows that requiring safety of outcome would nullify the expansive nature of the time, making it constrained. Finally, a second answer is given: the condition is explicitly conjecture of safety, which is not impossible and is within human capability.

Key Terms
شرط الجواز (shart al-jawaz): The condition that makes an action permissible (in this context, the condition to delay a wajib).
سلامة العاقبة (salamat al-aqibah): Safety of outcome; the actual fact that the obligated person will survive until the end of the time.
ظن السلامة (zann al-salamah): Conjecture or reasonable belief that one will be safe (from death) until the end of the time.
التخيير بين ممكن وممتنع (al-takhyir bayna mumkin wa mumtani'): A choice between a possible action (delay with conjecture) and an impossible action (delay with knowledge or actual safety).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Certain knowledge of safety from death.
B) Actual safety of outcome.
C) Conjecture (zann) of safety.
D) It is never permissible to delay.

Key Takeaway
The condition for delaying a broadly-timed wajib is conjecture (zann) of safety, not actual safety, otherwise the expansive time becomes meaningless.

The Hanafi Principle: Muqaddimah al-Wajib (Preliminary of an Obligation is Obligatory)
Presenting the issue of muqaddimah al-wajib and the positions on it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that muqaddimah al-wajib is wajib to identify necessary preliminaries of an obligation and state the rational proof.

Core Idea
The obligation of an act necessitates the obligation of its essential preliminaries; otherwise, commanding without means leads to impossibility.

This lesson explores the foundational usul rule that the preliminary (muqaddimah) of an obligatory act is itself obligatory. The Hanafi school holds this as a general principle, supported by rational proof and consensus. We will examine the classification of preliminaries into causes, conditions, and customary steps, and consider the differing opinions on this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
State the rule: muqaddimah al-wajib is wajib. Understand that 'preliminary' includes cause (sabab), condition (shart), and categories: shar'i, 'aqli, 'adi.
The text says 'mutaqliqan' – absolute obligation.
2
Classify the examples
From the text: wudu is a shar'i condition, leaving the opposite is 'aqli, washing part of the head is 'adi. Distinguish between necessary and optional preliminaries.
Customary preliminaries are those conventionally required for the act.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof
The text argues that if Allah commands an act without its preliminaries, it leads to commanding the impossible. Consensus supports that acquiring causes of wajib is wajib.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli) supported by ijma.
4
Map the ikhtilaf
Note the four positions: absolute obligation, limitation to sabab, limitation to shart shar'i, and no obligation. Recognize that the Hanafi view is the first.
The text states 'qila' for the alternative views without naming schools.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Given a new wajib, identify its necessary preliminaries and apply the rule to classify them as wajib.
Use the categories: sabab, shart, and the three types: shar'i, 'aqli, 'adi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة كون مقدمة الواجب واجبا والمذاهب فيه مقدمة الواجب المطلق واجب مطلقاً، أي سببا (كان) أو شرطاً، شرعاً (كان) كالوضوء أو عقلاً كترك الضد، أو عادة، كغسل جزء من الرأس لغسل الوجه، وقيل في السبب فقط، وقيل في الشرط الشرعي فقط وقيل لا وجوب مطلقاً لنا أن التكليف به بدون تكليف المقدمة يؤدى إلى التكليف بالمحال ألا ترى أن تحصيل أسباب الواجب واجب، وتحصيل أسباب الحرام حرام بالإجماع، وماقيل (إنه) يجوز أن يكون وجوبها لغيره كالإيمان، ففيه أن الكلام بالنظر إليه فإن قلت: لا يلزم الأمر صريحا قلت: لانزاع في ذلك، بل المراد أنه يستتبعه، وهو معنى قولهم: إيجاب المشروط إيجاب الشرط، ولهذا
Issue: The preliminary of an obligation is obligatory, and the positions on it. The absolute preliminary of the absolute obligation is absolutely obligatory, whether it is a cause or a condition, whether it is legal like wudu or rational like leaving a contradictory, or customary like washing part of the head for washing the face. And it is said (it is) only in the cause, and it is said only in the legal condition, and it is said not obligatory at all. Our proof: that the obligation of it without the obligation of the preliminary leads to the obligation of the impossible. Do you not see that the acquisition of causes of the obligatory is obligatory, and the acquisition of causes of the prohibited is prohibited by consensus. And what is said that it is possible that its obligation is for other than itself like iman, the response is that the discussion is with regard to it. If you say: the command is not required explicitly, I say: there is no dispute on that, but rather the intent is that it is entailed, and that is the meaning of their statement: the obligation of the conditional is the obligation of the condition. And therefore...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage defines a core usul rule: the prerequisites (muqaddimah) of an obligatory act are themselves obligatory. The author classifies muqaddimah into three types: sabab (cause), shart (condition), and three categories by nature: shar'i (legal, e.g., wudu for prayer), 'aqli (rational, e.g., abandoning contradictory actions), and 'adi (customary, e.g., washing part of the head for washing the face). Several opinions are recorded: some limit the obligation to causes only, some to legal conditions only, and others deny it entirely. The Hanafi proof is rational: if Allah commands something without commanding its necessary preliminaries, that would require the impossible (since the act cannot be performed without them). The author cites consensus that acquiring causes of the wajib is wajib and causes of haram are haram. The objection that the obligation of iman (faith) is for itself and not for its preliminaries is dismissed because the discussion concerns what follows necessarily. The text ends mid-argument, but the meaning is clear: the obligation of the conditional entails the obligation of its condition.

Key Terms
مقدمة الواجب (muqaddimah al-wajib): That which is necessary for the performance of a wajib act, whether cause, condition, or customary step.
سبب (sabab): A cause that must be brought into existence for the wajib to be possible (e.g., acquiring water for wudu).
شرط (shart): A condition that must be present for the wajib to be valid (e.g., wudu for prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof that the preliminary of a wajib is itself wajib?
A) Direct Quranic verse stating that all preliminaries are wajib.
B) Because commanding the wajib without its preliminaries would lead to commanding the impossible.
C) Consensus of the companions that every preliminary is wajib.
D) Because the preliminary is always explicitly mentioned in the text along with the wajib.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of an act necessarily entails the obligation of its necessary preliminaries; this principle is the foundation for deriving many practical rulings in Hanafi fiqh.

The Obligation of the Means to an Obligation: A Hanafi Usul Analysis
Exposition of three opinions on muqaddimat al-wajib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three positions on muqaddimat al-wajib and apply the Hanafi view that the means are obligatory incidentally (wajib bi al-'arad), distinguishing it from explicit obligation.

Core Idea
Commanding the conditioned entails commanding the condition, but the condition's obligation is incidental, not explicit.

This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut presents a foundational usul al-fiqh debate: when an act is obligatory, are its necessary prerequisites also obligatory? The discussion involves three schools of thought, a rational proof, and the crucial distinction between direct and incidental obligation. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to trace how obligations extend in the Hanafi legal system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the opening lines to extract the three positions: (1) only shar'i conditions are obligatory, (2) no prerequisites are obligatory, (3) all prerequisites are obligatory. Note the scope of each view.
The third view is the one defended by the Hanafi school in this passage.
2
Analyze the main proof for absolute obligation
Focus on the rational argument: if the main duty is commanded but its means are not, performing the duty becomes impossible, leading to taklif bi al-muhal, which is refuted by Quran 2:286. Also note the consensus on causes of the obligatory being obligatory.
This is the central dalil of the Hanafi position.
3
Evaluate the objection from faith
Consider the counterexample: faith is a prerequisite for all rulings but is obligatory for itself, not as a means. The text refutes this by restricting the discussion to prerequisites that are needed only for the specific duty, not for their own sake.
This clarifies that the obligation of a means is relative to the duty it facilitates.
4
Understand the incidental nature
Examine the distinction between wajib bi al-dhat (directly commanded) and wajib bi al-'arad (obligatory by entailment). The text states that the command attaches directly to the main duty, and the means follow incidentally.
This distinction explains why no explicit command is needed for the means.
5
Apply the practical consequences
Identify two specific results: (1) abandoning the means incurs only one sin (for the main duty), not multiple sins; (2) intention (niyyah) is not required for the means (e.g., wudu) because it is not an end in itself.
These consequences directly affect fiqh rulings on worship.

It was said: Rather its obligation is only in the case of a legal condition, like wudu for prayer, not a rational or customary condition. And it was said: There is no obligation absolutely, meaning the prerequisite is not obligatory, neither as a cause nor a condition nor anything else. The proof of those who affirm the obligation of the prerequisite absolutely: for us, the obligation of the duty without the obligation of the prerequisite leads to impossible obligation because the duty cannot be performed except through it. This is denied for two reasons: Allah said: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (Quran 2:286). Second: Do you not see that procuring the causes of an obligatory act is obligatory, and procuring the causes of a prohibited act is prohibited by consensus. And what was said in refutation of this proof—that the obligation of the prerequisite could be for its own sake, not for the sake of the duty, like the obligation of faith, which is a prerequisite for all secondary Islamic rulings yet faith is not obligatory for their sake but for its own sake—is problematic because the discussion about the obligation of the prerequisite is only with regard to the obligation and command of that for which it is a prerequisite, not regarding its obligation for itself or for something other than the commanded duty. The summary of the answer and refutation: If there is a duty and it has a prerequisite, and there is no other proof for the obligation of the prerequisite besides the dependence of that duty upon it, then that prerequisite is obligatory because otherwise performing that duty would be impossible, leading to an impossible command. [Another question on the ruling that the prerequisite is obligatory. If you say: The explicit command of obligation does not follow from the obligation of the prerequisite for the duty. We say: There is no dispute that the obligation of the prerequisite is not by an explicit command. Rather, the meaning is that the obligation of the duty entails the obligation of the prerequisite. This is the meaning of their statement: Commanding the conditioned (the duty) is commanding the condition (the prerequisite). For this reason, the conditions and causes that are prerequisites in themselves do not result in multiple sins for abandoning the duty; only one sin results for abandoning the original intended duty, not multiple sins for abandoning the many causes and conditions. The proof of those who deny the obligation of the prerequisite absolutely: They said: If the dependent (the prerequisite) were obligatory for the duty, then it would require conceiving the command that necessitates it (the dependent), because commanding something is impossible without conceiving it. This is incorrect, because often the commander commands something without conceiving its prerequisite (if it has no prerequisite). The author answered by saying: We say this is denied. Conception of the dependent is required only if the command is explicit and direct, like commanding the ruling itself. But if the command is incidental (by means of commanding something else), like commanding the dependent (the prerequisite) for the sake of the independent (the duty itself), then its conception is not required. Hence, because the prerequisite is obligatory by followership and incidentally, not intentionally, the address of the Lawgiver is not attached to the dependent itself (the prerequisite); rather, the address is attached to the duty intentionally and directly. So the duty in itself is the object of the address, not the incidental duty. And because the prerequisite is not obligatory in itself, there is no obligation of intention for it; intention is obligatory only for the duty in itself, not for the incidental duty, such as wudu as a prerequisite for prayer: its intention is not obligatory in itself such that the validity of prayer depends upon it.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents a classical usul al-fiqh debate on whether the means (muqaddimah) necessary to fulfill an obligatory act become obligatory themselves. The author, commenting on Musallam al-Thubut, expounds three positions: (1) Only shar'i conditions (like wudu for prayer) are obligatory, not rational or customary prerequisites. (2) The prerequisite is never obligatory. (3) The prerequisite is always obligatory. The main argument for the third position is rational: without making the means obligatory, the obligation of the main act would be impossible (تكليف بالمحال). This is rebutted by the Quranic verse 'لا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها' (Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity) and by the consensus that causes of the obligatory are obligatory and causes of the prohibited are prohibited. A further objection that the prerequisite might be obligatory for itself (like faith) is refuted: the discussion here concerns the obligation of the prerequisite due to its being a means to another duty, not its own inherent obligation. The conclusion is that the prerequisite is obligatory as a necessary consequence (إيجاب المشروط إيجاب الشرط), but this obligation is incidental, not by explicit command. Therefore, if one abandons the prerequisite, only one sin is incurred (for abandoning the main duty), not multiple sins. The deniers' argument that commanding a prerequisite requires conceiving it is countered: the command is incidental, so conception is not required. Finally, the incidental nature means that intention (niyyah) is not required for the prerequisite (like wudu), only for the main duty (like prayer).

Key Terms
مقدمة الواجب (muqaddimat al-wajib): An act or condition whose existence is necessary for performing an obligatory act.
الواجب بالذات (al-wajib bi al-dhat): An obligation that is commanded directly and intentionally, and its ruling is the object of the legislative address.
الواجب بالعرض (al-wajib bi al-'arad): An obligation that is not commanded directly but arises as a necessary consequence of another obligation.
تكليف بالمحال (taklif bi al-muhal): Commanding something impossible, which is not permissible in Islamic law.
إيجاب المشروط إيجاب الشرط (ijab al-mashrut ijab al-shart): The principle that commanding the conditioned (mashrut) entails commanding the condition (shart/means).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the Hanafi position that muqaddimat al-wajib are obligatory?
A) Explicit Quranic command for each means.
B) Consensus that all means are obligatory.
C) The rational necessity to avoid commanding the impossible.
D) The analogy with faith as a prerequisite.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the means to an obligation are obligatory incidentally, not by explicit command, based on the principle of ijab al-mashrut ijab al-shart and the avoidance of taklif bi al-muhal.

Sadd al-Dhara'i' Applied: Confusion of Halal and Haram
Furu' illustrating sadd al-dhara'i' in cases of ishtibah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that the means to an obligation are obligatory to cases of confusion (ishtibah) between halal and haram, and derive the ruling of precautionary prohibition.

Core Idea
When halal and haram are indistinguishable, both become prohibited — certainty of avoiding haram requires avoiding both.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text presents two practical branches (furu') that illustrate a foundational principle: the means to an obligation become obligatory. The author applies this principle to cases where a lawful wife is confused with a stranger and where a husband makes an ambiguous divorce statement. By working through these examples, you will learn how the Hanafi school uses ihtiyat (precaution) to ensure the avoidance of the unlawful, even at the cost of sacrificing the lawful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle being applied
The text says: 'because refraining from the unlawful is obligatory, and it is achieved by refraining from both.' So the principle is: avoiding haram is obligatory, and the means to achieve it are also obligatory.
2
Analyze the first furu' (confusion of wife and stranger)
Understand why both women become prohibited: since the stranger is haram, avoiding her is wajib; but since she cannot be identified, avoiding both is the only way to be certain.
3
Analyze the second furu' (ambiguous divorce statement)
If a man says 'one of my wives is divorced,' certainty of avoiding the unlawful (remaining married to a woman who is actually divorced) requires divorcing both, because he cannot know which one is divorced.
4
Recognize the common pattern
Both examples show that when certainty in distinguishing halal from haram is impossible, the precautionary obligation extends to both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفريع على أن مقدمة الواجب واجبة إذا شتبهت المنكوحة بالأجنبية حرمت؛ لأن الكف عن الحرام واجب وهو بالكف عنهما ولوقال: إحدى كما طالق حرمتا؛ لأن الاجتناب يقيناً فيه أقول : فالغاية داخلة في المغياء ليعلم وجود المغيا ١ - إذا اشتبهت المنكوحة بالأجنبية حرمت؛ لأن الكف عن الحرام واجب، وهو
Branches on the principle that the means to an obligation are obligatory: If a lawful wife becomes confused with a strange woman, both become prohibited, because refraining from the unlawful is obligatory, and it is achieved by refraining from both. And if he says: 'One of my wives is divorced,' both become divorced, because certain avoidance requires it. I say: The end is included in the means to know the existence of the end. 1 - If a lawful wife becomes confused with a strange woman, both become prohibited, because refraining from the unlawful is obligatory, and it is
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage provides two practical applications (furu') of the usul principle that the means to an obligation become obligatory. The first example: if a man's lawful wife is confused with a strange woman (i.e., he cannot distinguish between them), both women become prohibited to him. The reasoning: refraining from the unlawful (the strange woman) is obligatory. To achieve this certainty, he must refrain from both, because refraining from only one does not guarantee avoidance of the unlawful. The second example: if a man says 'One of my wives is divorced,' both become divorced. This is because certainty of avoiding the unlawful (remaining married to all wives while one is actually divorced) requires divorcing both. The commentator adds that the end (the obligation) is included in the means to achieve it. This illustrates how, in Hanafi fiqh, when halal and haram are mixed, ihtiyat (precaution) dictates avoiding both.

Key Terms
اشتباه (ishtibah): Confusion or ambiguity between lawful and unlawful such that one cannot differentiate.
المنكوحة (al-mankuhah): A woman married under a valid marriage contract.
الأجنبية (al-ajnabiyyah): A woman with whom marriage is not lawful at that moment (a non-mahram stranger).
الكف عن الحرام (al-kaf 'an al-haram): Abstaining from the prohibited act; an obligatory action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The stranger is prohibited but the wife remains halal.
B) Both are prohibited.
C) He must divorce both.
D) He may only approach the wife if he guesses correctly.

Key Takeaway
When halal and haram are indistinguishable, the Hanafi principle of sadd al-dhara'i' requires avoiding both to ensure the avoidance of the unlawful.

Obligation of Prerequisites and Its Branches
Discussing the obligation of prerequisites and applying it to cases of confusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to APPLY the Hanafi principle that prerequisites of obligatory acts are obligatory (wājib 'alā sabīl al-taba'iyyah) to derive rulings in cases of uncertainty (ishtibāh) and to distinguish between wājib bi al-dhāt and wājib bil-'arad.

Core Idea
A prerequisite is obligatory incidentally; the Lawgiver need not conceive it separately.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi principle that the prerequisites of an obligatory act themselves become obligatory, but only in an incidental (bil-'arad) sense. This subtle distinction has practical consequences, especially in cases of uncertainty. The text presents the proof, refutes the deniers, and then derives three branches that clarify how the principle operates in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Central Claim
Recognize that the author asserts that prerequisites of obligatory acts are themselves obligatory, but not independently—only derivatively (taba'an).
2
Understand the Opponents' Proof and Hanafi Response
The deniers argue that if a prerequisite were obligatory, the Lawgiver would have to conceive it. The author responds that the obligation is incidental (bil-'arad), not essential (bil-dhāt), so conception is not required.
The key phrase: 'قلنا: هذا ممنوع' marks the rebuttal.
3
Distinguish wājib bi al-dhāt and wājib bil-'arad
Note the difference: the primary obligatory act is wājib bi al-dhāt (directly intended by the Lawgiver), while the prerequisite is wājib bil-'arad (incidental, for the sake of the primary act).
This distinction explains why intention (niyyah) is not required for the prerequisite.
4
Apply to the First Two Branches
Consider the cases where a lawful wife is confused with a stranger, or a man says 'One of you is divorced' to his two wives. In both, avoiding the unlawful (a wājib) requires refraining from both, because the prerequisite (certainty of avoidance) is obligatory derivatively.
The principle forces a cautious ruling to ensure the primary obligation is fulfilled.
5
Apply to the Third Branch: Inclusion of the Ghāyah
The verse 'أتموا الصيام إلى الليل' shows that the ghāyah (limit, i.e., night) is a prerequisite for completing the fast. Thus, fasting a small part of the night becomes obligatory derivatively, ensuring the primary obligation is fully satisfied.
This demonstrates the principle's application to textual interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأسباب التي هي مقدمات لذاتها لايلزم في ترك الواجب إلا معصية واحدة بالنظر إلى ترك الواجب الأصلي المقصود، ولا يلزم المعاصي الكثيرة بالنظر إلى ترك الأسباب والشروط الكثيرة. دليل منكري وجوب المقدمة مطلقاً: قالوا المنكرون لوجوب المقدمة مطلقاً: لو وجب الموقوف عليه للواجب يعني المقدمة لزم تعقل تصور الأمر الموجب له للموقوف عليه لأن إيجاب شيء لا يمكن بدون تصوره، وهذا غير صحيح؛ لأنه كثيراً ما يحكم الآمر بشيء ولا يتصور مقدمته إذا لم يكن له مقدمة. وأجاب عنه المصنف بقوله: قلنا: هذا ممنوع، وإنما يلزم من إيجاب شيء تصوره تصور الموقوف عليه لو كان إيجابه صراحة وبالذات، كإيجاب نفس الحكم، وأما إذا كان إيجابه بالعرض بواسطة إيجاب شيء آخر كإيجاب الموقوف عليه المقدمة لأجل الموقوف نفس الواجب، فلا يلزم تصوره. ومن ههنا من أجل أن المقدمة واجبة تبعاً واستطراداً، دون قصداً لم يلزم تعلق خطاب الشارع بنفس الموقوف عليه المقدمة، وإنما يلزم تعلق الخطاب بالواجب قصداً وبالذات، فالواجب بالذات يكون متعلق الخطاب، دون الواجب بالعرض. ومن أجل أن المقدمة غير واجب بالذات لا وجوب نية لها، أي لا تكون النية واجبة لها، وإنما يكون وجوب النية للواجب بالذات دون الواجب بالعرض كالوضوء مقدمة للصلاة، وليست نيته واجباً بالذات حتى يتوقف عليه جوازها. التفريع على أن مقدمة الواجب واجبة: إذا اشتبهت المنكوحة بالأجنبية حرمت؛ لأن الكف عن الحرام واجب، وهو بالكف عنهما. ولو قال: إحداكما طالق حرمتا؛ لأن الاجتناب يقيناً فيه. أقول: فالغاية داخلة في المغيا ليعلم وجود المغيا. ١ - إذا اشتبهت المنكوحة بالأجنبية حرمت؛ لأن الكف عن الحرام واجب، وهو إنما يتحقق هنا بالكف عنهما عن المنكوحة والأجنبية. ٢- ولو قال لزوجتيه: إحداكما طالق، حرمتا؛ لأن الاجتناب عن الحرام يقيناً في أن كل منهما حرام جميعاً. ٣- الفرع الثالث لهذا الأصل مقدمة الواجب واجبة دخول الغاية في المغيا. أقول: فالغاية داخلة في المغيا ليعلم وجود المغيا بكامله. مثاله: قوله تعالى: ثم أتموا الصيام إلى الليل، فإن الغاية الليل كالمقدمة لصوم اليوم، فصوم جزء قليل من الليل وهو دقيقة أو ثانية منه لازم لاستيعاب كل اليوم الذي هو معيار للصوم، وإنما يحصل الاستيعاب الكامل إذا أحاط الصوم جميع أجزائه.
And the causes which are prerequisites in themselves: in abandoning the obligatory act only one sin is incurred with regard to abandoning the original intended obligatory act, and many sins are not incurred with regard to abandoning many causes and conditions. Proof of those who deny the obligation of the prerequisite absolutely: They, the deniers of the obligation of the prerequisite absolutely, said: If the thing on which something depends is obligatory for the obligatory act (meaning the prerequisite), then it would be necessary to conceive (visualize) the command that necessitates it (the dependent thing) because commanding something is impossible without conceiving it. This is incorrect because the commander often commands something without conceiving its prerequisite (if it has no prerequisite). The author replied to him by saying: We say: This is prevented; rather, the necessity of conceiving the dependent thing arises from commanding something explicitly and essentially, like commanding the ruling itself. But if the command is accidental (through commanding another thing), like commanding the dependent thing (the prerequisite) for the sake of the dependent (the obligatory act itself), then its conception is not necessary. From here (because the prerequisite is obligatory incidentally and derivatively, not intentionally), the address of the Lawgiver does not attach to the dependent thing itself (the prerequisite); rather, the address attaches to the obligatory act intentionally and essentially. So the essentially obligatory is the object of address, not the accidentally obligatory. And because the prerequisite is not essentially obligatory, there is no obligation of intention for it, i.e., intention is not obligatory for it; rather, obligation of intention applies to the essentially obligatory, not the accidentally obligatory, like ablution being a prerequisite for prayer — its intention is not essentially obligatory such that its validity depends on it. Branching on the principle that the prerequisite of an obligatory act is obligatory: (1) If the lawful wife is confused with a stranger, both become forbidden; because refraining from the unlawful is obligatory, and that is achieved here by refraining from both (the wife and the stranger). (2) And if he says to his two wives: 'One of you is divorced,' both become forbidden; because avoidance of the unlawful is certain in that each of them is unlawful together. (3) The third branch of this principle (the prerequisite of an obligatory act is obligatory): the inclusion of the ghāyah (limit) in the limited thing. I say: The ghāyah is included in the limited thing to known the existence of the limited thing in its entirety. Example: Allah's saying: 'Then complete the fast until the night.' Indeed, the ghāyah (the night) is like a prerequisite for the day's fast, so fasting a small part of the night (a minute or second of it) is necessary to encompass the entire day which is the standard for the fast; complete encompassment is only achieved when the fast covers all its parts.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and proof concerning the obligation of prerequisites (muqaddimah al-wājib). The author first clarifies that abandoning an obligatory act incurs only one sin, not multiple sins for abandoning its prerequisites, because the prerequisites are not independently obligated. The deniers of the obligation of the prerequisite argue that if a prerequisite were obligatory, the Lawgiver would have to conceive it, which is impossible. The author responds that the obligation of a prerequisite is incidental (bil-'arad), not explicit (bil-dhāt); the Lawgiver's address attaches directly to the obligatory act itself, and the prerequisite is obligated indirectly as a means. Therefore, the intention (niyyah) is not required for the prerequisite itself, as seen in wudū' for prayer. Then three branches are derived: (1) If a married woman is confused with a stranger, both become forbidden because avoiding the unlawful necessarily requires avoiding both. (2) If a man says to his two wives 'One of you is divorced,' both become forbidden because certainty of avoiding the unlawful demands refraining from both. (3) The ghāyah (limit) is included in the limited thing, as the night is included in the fast; a small part of the night must be fasted to ensure the entire day is covered, because the ghāyah is a prerequisite for fulfilling the obligation completely.

Key Terms
مقدمة الواجب (muqaddimah al-wājib): A prerequisite that is a means to fulfill an obligatory act; it is obligated incidentally (bil-'arad) and derivatively (taba'an).
واجب بالذات (wājib bi al-dhāt): An obligation that is intended directly by the Lawgiver; the primary object of command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his two wives 'One of you is divorced,' what is the ruling according to the Hanafi principle of muqaddimah al-wājib?
A) Neither is divorced until he specifies.
B) Both become forbidden for him.
C) Only one is divorced but he does not know which.
D) The marriage is annulled entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that prerequisites are obligatory derivatively (wājib bil-'arad) resolves the logical objection of the deniers and has concrete applications in cases of uncertainty and textual interpretation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Explicit and Implicit Address in the Prohibition of Opposites
Distinguishing explicit and implicit address in the context of opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between explicit and implicit address in the context of command and its opposite, and apply the rule that implicit address only yields karahah (dislike) while explicit address yields hurmah (prohibition).

Core Idea
The effect of a command on its opposite depends on whether the address is explicit or implicit.

In Hanafi usul, the ruling on the opposite of a commanded act is not absolute. The author distinguishes between explicit (sarih) and implicit (dhimni) address. An explicit command clearly prohibits its opposite, making it haram. An implicit command only indicates karahah, not prohibition. This nuance is crucial for avoiding overextension of the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of address
The text mentions explicit (sarih) and implicit (dhimni) address. Each yields a different ruling for the opposite: prohibition vs. dislike.
Implicit address is weaker and only indicates karahah.
2
Understand the limitation of the principle
Command does not always prohibit the opposite; it only does so when the address is explicit.
3
Analyze the question and answer about contradiction
The question posits that commanding something prohibits every specific opposite, leading to a contradiction. The answer resolves it using the concept of different respects (jihaat).
Lying down is possible in itself but impossible due to the command to stand.
4
Apply the concept of different respects
Something can be possible in itself but impossible due to another obligation. This is key in Hanafi usul for resolving apparent conflicts.
The contradiction is only apparent, not real.

إليه من حيث هو هو. وحاصل الجواب عن الاعتراض الأول أنه لا يقال في الأشكال كذلك لأنا نقول في رد الاعتراض المبنى على القانون الاول إن الأمر لا يقتضي
It includes two types that are mutually exclusive, one does not apply to the other. The implicit address only indicates dislike of the opposite, not prohibition. The gist is that commanding something establishes dislike of its opposite, not prohibition, due to the weakness of the address indicating the consequential ruling, while the opposite of the obligatory and its preventer is the prohibited, not the disliked. The summary of the answer: The opposite of the obligatory established by command may be prohibited if that opposite is established by explicit address, and may be disliked if established by implicit address. The question and its answer: If you say that commanding something is a prohibition of every specific opposite, and prohibition of the opposite necessitates commanding another opposite by choice (between two things: either the commanded or the prohibited), then this opposite is prohibited specifically and commanded by choice—this is a contradiction (and it is fit to be dismissed due to its falsehood and ugliness). Then the author clarified this question in his commentary and said: The formulation of the question: If we assume standing is commanded, then that necessitates that each of sitting and lying down is prohibited specifically, by virtue of the apparent command implying prohibition of opposites. So prohibition of sitting necessitates commanding lying down by choice, by virtue of that prohibition in general necessitates commanding one of the opposites. Thus lying down is prohibited specifically and commanded by choice, so lying down is both impossible and permissible. The formulation of the answer: Prevention of impossibility in combining the impossible and the permissible if they are from two different respects. That is because the possibility of lying down with respect to the prohibition of sitting does not negate its impossibility by essence, if it is assumed to be prohibited in itself as well, nor its impossibility with respect to another thing, which is standing. So generalizing impossibility is an expansion in the answer, meaning impossibility is of two types: by essence and with respect to another. So its possibility with respect to its essence and its impossibility with respect to another, which is standing. And the author answered that question in the text: Thus I said in the answer: Possibility with respect to one thing does not negate impossibility by essence, nor impossibility with respect to another thing. So in our case, lying down can be possible by virtue of the prohibition of sitting and by virtue of its essence, and can be impossible with respect to standing. It is only impossible to combine the impossible and the permissible if from one respect; if from two respects, it is fine. The two objections based on the two preceding principles: The first principle: The obligation of a thing and the command for it entail the prohibition of its opposite. The objection based on it: On the basis of this principle, it would entail the prohibition of obligatory acts, such as the prohibition of prayer because it is an opposite of Hajj. So if Hajj is commanded, prayer becomes its opposite and is prohibited and forbidden, and vice versa. Both are obligatory duties of the religion and pillars of Islam. The meaning of opposition is that one is not performed with the actions of the other. The second principle: Prohibition of something entails commanding its opposite and its obligation. The objection based on this principle: It would entail the obligation of prohibited acts, even if by choice between two prohibited things (meaning prevention from one of them not specifically determined), such as the obligation of fornication because it is the abandonment of sodomy at the time of committing fornication. For the prohibition of sodomy necessitates commanding its opposite, which is fornication (because they are two opposite actions with respect to the locus at one time). So it would necessitate the obligation of fornication from the prohibition of its opposite, sodomy, so the forbidden fornication becomes obligatory. And this is an explanation of his statement: It should not be said: On the first principle follows the prohibition of obligations, such as prohibition of prayer because it is opposite of Hajj, and vice versa; and on the second principle follows the obligation of forbidden acts, even if by choice, such as the obligation of fornication because it is leaving sodomy, and vice versa. Then he began to answer it and said: For we say concerning the first: The obligation of a thing entails the prohibition of its opposite; the command (which indicates obligation) does not entail comprehensiveness, so commanding something is not a prohibition of the opposite always. So it is possible to perform its opposite obligatory at another time. From here it is said: The condition is that the obligatory is time-restricted. But it follows from this that the time of Hajj is not 'Umrah, unless it is said that its time is considered from its own perspective. The gist of the answer to the first objection: It is not said in such forms. For we say in refuting the objection based on the first principle that the command does not entail...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the implications of command (amr) and prohibition (nahy) regarding their opposites (addad). The author first distinguishes between explicit and implicit address: implicit command only implies dislike (karahah) of the opposite, not legal prohibition (hurmah). However, the opposite of an obligatory act is usually haram, but here it may be only makruh if the address is implicit. Then a question is raised: if commanding something prohibits every specific opposite, and prohibiting an opposite commands another opposite by choice, then something could be both prohibited and commanded simultaneously—a contradiction. The answer resolves this by noting that possibility and impossibility can coexist from different respects. For example, lying down may be possible with respect to the prohibition of sitting but impossible with respect to the command to stand. The same thing can be possible by its essence and impossible due to another consideration. The author then presents two objections based on two principles. First: if commanding something entails prohibiting its opposite, then obligatory acts like salat and hajj would be prohibited when one is commanded, which is absurd because both are obligations. Second: if prohibiting something entails commanding its opposite, then zina (fornication) would become obligatory because it is the opposite of liwat (sodomy), which is prohibited. The beginning of the answer states that command does not always prohibit the opposite comprehensively; the opposite can be performed at another time. The condition is that the obligatory act be time-restricted (mudayyaq), but this leads to further discussion. The passage ends mid-answer, but the gist is that the implications of amr and nahy are not absolute but depend on context and time constraints.

Key Terms
الخطاب الضمني (al-khitab al-dhimni): The implicit address that does not explicitly state the ruling but is inferred from context.
الضد (al-didd): The opposite of a commanded or prohibited act; something that cannot coexist in the same time or locus.
الممتنع (al-mumtani'): Something impossible to exist due to a legal impediment, either by essence or with respect to another thing.
الجائز (al-ja'iz): Something permissible, legally allowed to exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the implicit address (khitab dhimni) produce for the opposite of a commanded act?
A) Prohibition (hurmah)
B) Dislike (karahah)
C) Obligation (wujub)
D) Permissibility (ibahah)

Key Takeaway
The effect of a command on its opposite is not absolute; it depends on whether the address is explicit (yields prohibition) or implicit (yields only dislike).

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate

Two Objections to the Principles of Command and Prohibition
Presenting and refuting two objections to the principles about opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two objections to the principles that command prohibits opposite and prohibition commands opposite, and explain the Hanafi response that command does not require comprehensiveness.

Core Idea
The principle that command prohibits opposite does not make obligatory acts haram because command does not require isti'ab (comprehensiveness).

This passage presents two objections that arise from the principles: (1) if commanding prohibits opposite, then obligatory acts like prayer and Hajj would be haram; (2) if prohibiting commands opposite, then haram acts like fornication would become obligatory. The Hanafi response begins by stating that command does not demand comprehensiveness, so the opposite can be performed at another time.

Guided Path
1
State the first principle and its objection
First principle: commanding something entails prohibition of its opposite. Objection: This would make obligatory acts (e.g., prayer) haram because they are opposites of other obligations (e.g., Hajj).
Both prayer and Hajj are obligatory, so this consequence would be absurd.
2
State the second principle and its objection
Second principle: prohibition of something entails commanding its opposite. Objection: This would make haram acts (e.g., fornication) obligatory because they are opposites of other haram acts (e.g., sodomy).
Fornication is haram, but it would become wajib if this principle were absolute.
3
Understand the Hanafi response to the first objection
The author says that command does not require isti'ab (comprehensiveness), so it is possible to perform the opposite at another time. Thus, prayer and Hajj can be performed at different times without conflict.
The principle only applies when the commanded act is time-restricted (mudayyaq).
4
Note the beginning of the answer
The passage only begins the answer, but the key point is that the command does not prohibit the opposite always; it only does so if the opposite cannot be performed at another time due to obligation.
Further explanation would continue on the next page.

The summary of the answer: The opposite of the obligatory established by command may be prohibited if that opposite is established by explicit address, and may be disliked if established by implicit address. The question and its answer: If you say that commanding something is a prohibition of every specific opposite, and prohibition of the opposite necessitates commanding another opposite by choice (between two things: either the commanded or the prohibited), then this opposite is prohibited specifically and commanded by choice—this is a contradiction (and it is fit to be dismissed due to its falsehood and ugliness). Then the author clarified this question in his commentary and said: The formulation of the question: If we assume standing is commanded, then that necessitates that each of sitting and lying down is prohibited specifically, by virtue of the apparent command implying prohibition of opposites. So prohibition of sitting necessitates commanding lying down by choice, by virtue of that prohibition in general necessitates commanding one of the opposites. Thus lying down is prohibited specifically and commanded by choice, so lying down is both impossible and permissible. The formulation of the answer: Prevention of impossibility in combining the impossible and the permissible if they are from two different respects. That is because the possibility of lying down with respect to the prohibition of sitting does not negate its impossibility by essence, if it is assumed to be prohibited in itself as well, nor its impossibility with respect to another thing, which is standing. So generalizing impossibility is an expansion in the answer, meaning impossibility is of two types: by essence and with respect to another. So its possibility with respect to its essence and its impossibility with respect to another, which is standing. And the author answered that question in the text: Thus I said in the answer: Possibility with respect to one thing does not negate impossibility by essence, nor impossibility with respect to another thing. So in our case, lying down can be possible by virtue of the prohibition of sitting and by virtue of its essence, and can be impossible with respect to standing. It is only impossible to combine the impossible and the permissible if from one respect; if from two respects, it is fine. The two objections based on the two preceding principles: The first principle: The obligation of a thing and the command for it entail the prohibition of its opposite. The objection based on it: On the basis of this principle, it would entail the prohibition of obligatory acts, such as the prohibition of prayer because it is an opposite of Hajj. So if Hajj is commanded, prayer becomes its opposite and is prohibited and forbidden, and vice versa. Both are obligatory duties of the religion and pillars of Islam. The meaning of opposition is that one is not performed with the actions of the other. The second principle: Prohibition of something entails commanding its opposite and its obligation. The objection based on this principle: It would entail the obligation of prohibited acts, even if by choice between two prohibited things (meaning prevention from one of them not specifically determined), such as the obligation of fornication because it is the abandonment of sodomy at the time of committing fornication. For the prohibition of sodomy necessitates commanding its opposite, which is fornication (because they are two opposite actions with respect to the locus at one time). So it would necessitate the obligation of fornication from the prohibition of its opposite, sodomy, so the forbidden fornication becomes obligatory. And this is an explanation of his statement: It should not be said: On the first principle follows the prohibition of obligations, such as prohibition of prayer because it is opposite of Hajj, and vice versa; and on the second principle follows the obligation of forbidden acts, even if by choice, such as the obligation of fornication because it is leaving sodomy, and vice versa. Then he began to answer it and said: For we say concerning the first: The obligation of a thing entails the prohibition of its opposite; the command (which indicates obligation) does not entail comprehensiveness, so commanding something is not a prohibition of the opposite always. So it is possible to perform its opposite obligatory at another time. From here it is said: The condition is that the obligatory is time-restricted. But it follows from this that the time of Hajj is not 'Umrah, unless it is said that its time is considered from its own perspective. The gist of the answer to the first objection: It is not said in such forms. For we say in refuting the objection based on the first principle that the command does not entail...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the implications of command (amr) and prohibition (nahy) regarding their opposites (addad). The author first distinguishes between explicit and implicit address: implicit command only implies dislike (karahah) of the opposite, not legal prohibition (hurmah). However, the opposite of an obligatory act is usually haram, but here it may be only makruh if the address is implicit. Then a question is raised: if commanding something prohibits every specific opposite, and prohibiting an opposite commands another opposite by choice, then something could be both prohibited and commanded simultaneously—a contradiction. The answer resolves this by noting that possibility and impossibility can coexist from different respects. For example, lying down may be possible with respect to the prohibition of sitting but impossible with respect to the command to stand. The same thing can be possible by its essence and impossible due to another consideration. The author then presents two objections based on two principles. First: if commanding something entails prohibiting its opposite, then obligatory acts like salat and hajj would be prohibited when one is commanded, which is absurd because both are obligations. Second: if prohibiting something entails commanding its opposite, then zina (fornication) would become obligatory because it is the opposite of liwat (sodomy), which is prohibited. The beginning of the answer states that command does not always prohibit the opposite comprehensively; the opposite can be performed at another time. The condition is that the obligatory act be time-restricted (mudayyaq), but this leads to further discussion. The passage ends mid-answer, but the gist is that the implications of amr and nahy are not absolute but depend on context and time constraints.

Key Terms
الضد (al-didd): The opposite of an act; something that cannot coexist in the same time/locus.
الاستيعاب (al-isti'ab): Comprehensiveness; the quality of a command that covers all times or individuals.
المضيق (al-mudayyaq): Time-restricted obligatory act with a specific timeframe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why does commanding Hajj not prohibit prayer?
A) Because prayer is not a true opposite of Hajj.
B) Because the command does not require comprehensiveness, so prayer can be performed at another time.
C) Because prayer is a higher obligation.
D) Because the principle only applies to non-essential acts.

Key Takeaway
Command does not require comprehensiveness (isti'ab), so the opposite of a commanded act can be performed at another time without conflict.

The Command Does Not Absolutely Prohibit Its Opposite – Only During Performance
Defining the temporal scope of the implied prohibition of the opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that amr bi shay' implies nahy an didd only during the time of performance, distinguishing between restricted (mudayyaq) and expanded (musawwa') obligations.

Core Idea
A command only prohibits its opposite while you are doing the commanded act, not forever.

This passage refines a key usul principle: does commanding something imply prohibition of its opposite? The Hanafi position is nuanced: the prohibition is temporally limited to the moment of performing the commanded act. We explore examples like Hajj and prayer, and the distinction between restricted and expanded obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the initial claim
Identify the general claim that amr bi shay' implies nahy an didd. Then note the Hanafi refinement: it is not absolute but limited to the time of performance.
The text says: 'فالأمر بأداء الحج لا يكون نهياً عن الصلاة في جميع أوقات الحج، بل نهى عن الصلاة في أوقات أداء مناسك الحج'.
2
Analyze the Hajj-prayer example
Work through the example: command to perform Hajj does not prohibit prayer in all times of Hajj (the whole season), only during the actual rites. Similarly, prayer prohibits Hajj only during prayer time.
This shows the temporal limitation.
3
Examine the objection about expanded obligations
Consider the objection: if the time for Hajj is the whole lifetime (wajib musawwa'), then prayer would be prohibited for life. The answer: the whole lifetime is considered time for Hajj only in the sense that it can be done any year, not that every moment is exclusively for Hajj.
The text distinguishes between 'al-wajib al-mudayyaq' and 'al-wajib al-musawwa'.
4
Identify the condition for the rule
Conclude that the rule that amr implies nahy applies fully only when the wajib is mudayyaq (restricted). For musawwa', the prohibition is limited to the specific performance times.
This is the core Hanafi position in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استيعاب جميع أوقات المأمور به حتى لا يمكن الإتيان بضده في تلك الأوقات، فلا يكون في الأمر بشيء النهي عن ضده دائماً، بل يقتضي الأمر بشيء النهي عن ضده في أوقات أداء المأمور به لا في جميع الأوقات، فيمكن أداء الواجب ضد المأمور به في وقت آخر غير وقت المأمور به، فالأمر بأداء الحج لا يكون نهياً عن الصلاة في جميع أوقات الحج، بل نهى عن الصلاة في أوقات أداء مناسك الحج، وأما في وقت آخر فأداء الصلاة ليس ممنوعاً ومنهياً عنه، وكذلك الأمر بالصلاة يقتضي النهي عن الحج في أوقات أداء الصلاة، لا في جميع الأوقات، حتى لا يمكن أداء الحج.
وأما الإيمان أولاً فهو من قسم الإدراك الذي معه حكم، فوجوبه لا ينافي سائر الأفعال الواجبة، وثانياً أن الإيمان هو الشرط الأول لجميع الواجبات، فلا يكون ضداً لها. ثم فرع المصنف على هذا الجواب وقال: ومن ههنا أي ومن أجل أن الأمر بالشيء لا يقتضي استيعاب جميع أوقات المأمور به، فيمكن الإتيان بضده في وقت آخر قيل: إن الشرط في تضمن الأمر الموجب حرمة الضد أن يكون الواجب الثابت بذلك الأمر مضيقاً بحيث لا يمكن أداء الضد في وقت ذلك الواجب، وأما الواجب الموسع الذي يمكن في وقته أداء الواجب وأداء ضده، فأمر ذلك الواجب لا يتضمن حرمة الضد. ولما ورد على هذا الجواب إشكال بأنه إذا كان الإتيان بضد الواجب في وقته ممنوعاً يلزم أن لا يكون العمر جميع وقت الحج؛ لأنه لو كان جميع العمر وقتاً للحج فيصير ضده وهو الصلاة ممنوعاً طول العمر، وهو كما ترى أجاب عنه بقوله : إلا أن يقال ذلك (طول العمر) وقته نظراً إليه من حيث أنه حج مع قطع النظر عن كونه ضداً للصلاة، وبهذا الاعتبار واللحاظ لا يكون ضداً للصلاة. وأجاب الفقهاء بأن كل العمر ليس وقتاً للحج بطريق الاستيعاب، بل المراد أن وقت الحج ليس خاصاً بوقت دون وقت، وعام دون عام، بل أي عام أراد الحج جاز له. (٢) وحاصل الجواب عن الإشكال الثاني: إنا نقول في جواب الاعتراض المبني على القانون الثاني أن النهي عن شيء يقتضي وجوب ضده تخييراً) إن هذه القاعدة تكون وتجري في محل لا يكون فيه دليل أصلي على حرمة ضد المنهي عنه، وهنا الزنا واللواطة وإن كانا ضدين، ولكن ورد على حرمة كل واحد منهما دليل أصلي مستقل من الكتاب والسنة، فهذا الدليل أخرج المحل (الزنا واللواطة) عن قبول الوجوب التخييري تبعاً، أي بمتابعة دلالة النهي على وجوب الضد.
The comprehensiveness of all times of the commanded act so that its opposite cannot be performed in those times, so a command of a thing is not always a prohibition of its opposite. Rather, commanding a thing necessitates the prohibition of its opposite in the times of performing the commanded act, not in all times. So it is possible to perform an obligatory act opposite to the commanded act at another time other than the time of the commanded act. The command to perform Hajj is not a prohibition of prayer in all times of Hajj, but it prohibits prayer during the times of performing the rites of Hajj. As for another time, performing prayer is not prohibited and forbidden. Similarly, the command to pray necessitates the prohibition of Hajj during the times of performing prayer, not in all times, so that Hajj cannot be performed. As for faith, firstly, it is of the type of perception with which there is a ruling, so its obligation does not contradict other obligatory acts. Secondly, faith is the first condition for all obligations, so it is not opposite to them. Then the author branched on this answer and said: From here, i.e., because commanding a thing does not necessitate comprehensiveness of all times of the commanded act, so it is possible to perform its opposite at another time, it was said: The condition for the command that necessitates the prohibition of the opposite is that the obligation established by that command be restricted so that the opposite cannot be performed during the time of that obligation. As for the expanded obligation during the time of which both the obligation and its opposite can be performed, the command of that obligation does not include the prohibition of the opposite. When an objection was raised against this answer that if performing the opposite of the obligation during its time is prohibited, then it would follow that the whole lifetime is not the time for Hajj; because if the whole lifetime were the time for Hajj, then its opposite, prayer, would be prohibited for the entire lifetime, which is as you see. He answered it by saying: Except that it can be said that that (the whole lifetime) is its time considering it as Hajj, regardless of its being opposite to prayer. With this consideration and regard, it is not opposite to prayer. And the jurists answered that the whole lifetime is not the time for Hajj by way of comprehensiveness; rather, the meaning is that the time for Hajj is not specific to a particular time or a particular year. Rather, whichever year he intends to perform Hajj, it is permissible for him. (2) The summary of the answer to the second objection: We say in response to the objection based on the second principle that 'prohibition of a thing necessitates the obligation of its opposite in a permissive sense' that this rule applies and runs in a place where there is no original evidence for the prohibition of the opposite of the prohibited thing. Here, zina and liwat, although they are opposites, yet an independent original evidence from the Book and Sunnah has come for the prohibition of each one of them. So this evidence removes the case (zina and liwat) from accepting the permissive obligation as a follow-up, i.e., by following the implication of the prohibition on the obligation of the opposite.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage discusses a key usul rule: does commanding something imply prohibition of its opposite? The author refines the general claim: command does not imply blanket prohibition of the opposite at all times, but only during the time when the commanded act is to be performed. For example, the command to perform Hajj prohibits prayer only during the actual performance of Hajj rites, not at all times of the year. Similarly, prayer prohibits Hajj only during prayer time. The author then addresses two objections. First, about iman (faith): it is not considered an opposite because it is a prerequisite for all obligations. Second, concerning expanded obligations (wajib musawwa') like Hajj: if the obligation is expansive (time for Hajj extends over the whole life), then its opposite (prayer) would be prohibited all the time; but the answer is that the whole lifetime is considered the time for Hajj only in the sense that it can be done any year, not that every moment is for Hajj. Finally, regarding the principle that prohibition of something necessitates the obligation of its opposite (permissive obligation), the author notes that this principle only applies when there is no independent evidence prohibiting the opposite. For zina and liwat, independent proofs exist, so they are not subject to this derived obligation.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): The command of the Lawgiver that demands action from the obligated person.
الضد (al-didd): An act that is mutually exclusive with the commanded act such that both cannot be performed together.
الواجب المضيق (al-wajib al-mudayyaq): An obligation with a limited time frame that cannot accommodate its opposite.
الواجب الموسع (al-wajib al-musawwa'): An obligation with a broad time frame that can accommodate its opposite at other times.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what does the command to perform Hajj imply regarding salah (prayer)?
A) It prohibits salah at all times of the year.
B) It prohibits salah only during the specific performance of Hajj rites.
C) It has no effect on the ruling of salah.
D) It makes salah invalid for the entire year if one intends Hajj.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that a command implies prohibition of its opposite only during the actual performance of the commanded act, not at all times.

When Prohibition Does Not Imply Obligation of the Opposite – The Exception for Independently Prohibited Acts
Explaining the exceptional limitation to the principle that prohibition implies permissive obligation of the opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the principle 'prohibition implies permissive obligation of the opposite' is overridden by independent textual evidence, as in the examples of zina and liwat.

Core Idea
If a prohibited act has its own independent evidence, the prohibition of its opposite does not create a new obligation.

This passage addresses a second objection built on the principle that prohibition of a thing implies the obligation of its opposite in a permissive sense (wujub al-didd takhyiran). The author clarifies that this principle only applies when there is no independent proof for the prohibition of the opposite. For acts like zina and liwat, which are independently prohibited by the Quran and Sunnah, the rule does not apply.

Guided Path
1
State the principle of prohibition implying permissive obligation of the opposite
The legal maxim: 'al-nahy an al-shay' yufidu wujub al-didd takhyiran' – prohibition of a thing necessitates the obligation of its opposite in a permissive sense. This means if one is prohibited from doing X, one must choose from among the permissible acts that are opposite to X.
This is a Hanafi usul principle.
2
Identify the condition for the principle to apply
The principle applies only when there is no original evidence (dalil asli) prohibiting the opposite act itself. If the opposite is independently prohibited, then the principle does not generate a new obligation.
The text says: 'تكون وتجري في محل لا يكون فيه دليل أصلي على حرمة ضد المنهي عنه'.
3
Apply the condition to zina and liwat
Zina and liwat are opposites to many commanded acts (like marriage or modesty), but they are already independently prohibited by the Quran and Sunnah. Therefore, the prohibition of their opposites (e.g., the command to marry) does not create an obligation to commit zina/liwat? Actually, the opposite of prohibition of zina is the obligation to avoid it, but the principle would make avoiding zina obligatory anyway. The passage's point: the prohibition of zina itself already gives that ruling; the principle of 'nahy implies wujub al-didd' is not needed and does not apply because there is an independent dalil.
This protects against double counting and ensures the independent evidence governs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage discusses a key usul rule: does commanding something imply prohibition of its opposite? The author refines the general claim: command does not imply blanket prohibition of the opposite at all times, but only during the time when the commanded act is to be performed. For example, the command to perform Hajj prohibits prayer only during the actual performance of Hajj rites, not at all times of the year. Similarly, prayer prohibits Hajj only during prayer time. The author then addresses two objections. First, about iman (faith): it is not considered an opposite because it is a prerequisite for all obligations. Second, concerning expanded obligations (wajib musawwa') like Hajj: if the obligation is expansive (time for Hajj extends over the whole life), then its opposite (prayer) would be prohibited all the time; but the answer is that the whole lifetime is considered the time for Hajj only in the sense that it can be done any year, not that every moment is for Hajj. Finally, regarding the principle that prohibition of something necessitates the obligation of its opposite (permissive obligation), the author notes that this principle only applies when there is no independent evidence prohibiting the opposite. For zina and liwat, independent proofs exist, so they are not subject to this derived obligation.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A demand for abstinence from an act.
الوجوب التخييري (al-wujub al-takhyiri): A permissive obligation where the obligated person can choose from among several acts.
الدليل الأصلي (al-dalil al-asli): An original, independent source of evidence (Quran or Sunnah) that establishes a ruling directly, not via implication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the prohibition of zina not imply a permissive obligation of its opposite?
A) Because zina is not a prohibited act.
B) Because there is independent evidence prohibiting zina.
C) Because the opposite of zina is not clearly defined.
D) Because the principle only applies to acts of worship.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that 'prohibition implies permissive obligation of the opposite' has an exception: it does not apply when the opposite is independently prohibited by primary texts.

The Five Meanings of Ja'iz in Hanafi Usul al-Fiqh
Enumerates the five meanings of ja'iz in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five distinct meanings of the term 'ja'iz' as used in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
The term 'ja'iz' in usul has five distinct meanings, ranging from mubah to rationally possible, and includes even wajib and doubtful acts.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the term 'ja'iz' is commonly used, but its precise meaning depends on context. This passage from a commentary on Musallam al-Thubut lays out five distinct meanings, from the narrow sense of 'permissible' to broader senses that include obligatory actions and even doubtful matters. Understanding these nuances is crucial for correctly interpreting rulings and scholarly discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five meanings listed
The author enumerates five uses: (1) mubah, (2) not prohibited (including wajib/mandub), (3) rationally possible, (4) equal in act and omission (legally or rationally), (5) doubtful due to conflicting evidence.
2
Understand the scope of each meaning
Note that the second meaning (opposite of haram) is broader than the first, and the fourth includes ibaha plus rational equality. The fifth covers both legal and rational doubt.
3
Examine the commentator's refinements
The commentator adds that the first meaning is 'specific legal possibility' and distinguishes rational possibility as not part of fiqh usage. He also gives examples: urine of donkey (legal doubt) and action of a child (rational indifference).
4
Apply the classification
Given a statement using 'ja'iz', determine which meaning is intended based on context. For example, 'Selling is ja'iz' might mean mubah or not prohibited, depending on whether obligation is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معانى لفظ الجائز) الخمسة
اعلم أن الجائز كما يطلق على المباح يطلق على ما لا يمتنع شرعاً، وعلى ما لا يمتنع عقلاً ، و على ما استوى الأمران فيه شرعاً أو عقلاً ، وعلى المشكوك فيه كذلك ، هذا
اعلم أن ( الجائز) كما يطلق (۱) على المباح ، (۲) بيطلق على مالا يمتنع شرعاً، أي يطلق على ضد الحرام، فيشمل الواجب والمندوب والمباح ، فصار الواجب بهذا الاعتبار نوعاً من الجواز (۳) و يطلق على مالا يمتنع عقلاً، أي على مقابل الممتنع عقلاً، وهو الممكن العقلى ، (٤) و يطلق على ما استوى (فيه) الأمران (الفعل والترك شرعاً أو عقلاً، وهذا أعم من الإباحة ، فان الإباحة فيه الاستواء شرعاً فقط، وهذا الاستواء شرعاً و عقلاً، وبما أنه استوى فيه الأمر ان عقلاً، فيشمل المشكوك فيه عقلاً، مثل الأمور العقلية التي تعارضت فيها الأدلة العقلية ، (٥) وكذلك يطلق على المشكوك فيه ( شرعاً) لتعارض الأدلة الشرعية فيه، كسور الحمار، هذا، خذ هذا.

ه والخامس المشكوك فيه في نظر المجتهد شرعاً، كما في تعارض دليلين، أو عقلاً
The five meanings of the term 'ja'iz'. Know that 'ja'iz' is used for mubah (permissible), for that which is not prohibited legally, for that which is not impossible rationally, for that in which both (act and omission) are equal legally or rationally, and for the doubtful likewise. This. Know that 'ja'iz' is used (1) for mubah, (2) for that which is not prohibited legally, i.e., opposite of haram, so it includes wajib, mandub, and mubah; thus wajib becomes a type of jawaz in this consideration. (3) It is used for that which is not impossible rationally, i.e., opposite of rationally impossible, which is the rationally possible. (4) It is used for that in which both aspects (act and omission) are equal legally or rationally. This is more general than ibaha, because ibaha has equality only legally, while this has equality legally and rationally; and since the two aspects are equal rationally, it includes what is doubtful rationally, like intellectual matters where rational evidence conflict. (5) Similarly, it is used for the doubtful (legally) due to conflicting legal evidence, like the urine of a donkey. This. The author said in his commentary on this passage: Thus, ja'iz has meanings: First, the specific legal possibility; as for the specific rational possibility, among its meanings in intellectual matters, it is not among the jurists' usage. Second, opposite of haram. Third, opposite of impossible. Fourth, that which does not involve benefit or harm in existence or non-existence, not legally like mubah, nor rationally like the action of a child. Fifth, the doubtful from the viewpoint of the mujtahid legally, as when two evidences conflict, or rationally.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition explaining the polysemy of 'ja'iz,' a term commonly used in fiqh and usul. The author presents five meanings: (1) permissible (mubah), the narrowest sense; (2) not prohibited legally, which is broader and includes wajib, mandub, and mubah; (3) not impossible rationally, i.e., logically possible; (4) equal in act and omission either legally or rationally, broader than ibaha; (5) doubtful due to conflicting evidence (either legal or rational). The commentator adds a clarification: the first meaning 'specific legal possibility' (imkan khass shar'i) is the jurists' typical usage; rational possibility is not part of fiqh usage. The second meaning is opposite of haram; third is opposite of impossible; fourth is an act that lacks both benefit and harm (neither legally nor rationally); fifth is the doubtful in the view of the mujtahid. The examples help distinguish: 'urine of a donkey' is legally doubtful due to conflicting evidence; the action of a child is rationally indifferent (no rational benefit/harm). This classification is crucial for understanding how rulings are categorized.

Key Terms
الجائز (al-ja'iz): A term with five distinct meanings: mubah, non-prohibited (including obligatory), rationally possible, legally/rationally indifferent, and doubtful.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Legal permissibility with equality of act and omission only legally, not rationally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The first meaning (mubah).
B) The second meaning (opposite of haram).
C) The third meaning (not impossible rationally).
D) The fourth meaning (equal in act and omission).

Key Takeaway
The term 'ja'iz' in Hanafi usul has five distinct meanings, and correctly identifying which is intended is essential for precise legal understanding.
